What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Defending the Subject of Kalam as Existent Qua Existent
The author defends the subject of kalam as existent qua existent against objections from mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the definition of kalam's subject as 'existent qua existent' against the objection that kalam discusses non-existents.

Core Idea
Discussions of non-existents are either principles or subsidiaries, not issues, preserving the subject.

When defining kalam as the science of existent qua existent, an obvious objection arises: mutakallimun frequently discuss things that have no external existence, like 'consideration' and 'proof', or non-existents like 'the non-existent' and 'state'. This passage presents the objection and the author's nuanced defense, distinguishing between the principles and issues of kalam, and addressing the question of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objection raised in al-Mawaqif against the subject of kalam being existent qua existent. Notice how it points to discussions of things without existence.
2
Understand the initial answer
The author responds by distinguishing between principles (mabadi) and issues (masa'il). Discussions of nazar and dalil are principles, not issues. Discussions of ma'dum and hal are subsidiaries of the issue of existence.
3
Examine the clarifying addition
The author further explains that examples like the restoration of the non-existent, infinite regress, and hyle actually relate to states of the existent. He clarifies that 'existent' is taken absolutely, including mental existence.
4
Reflect on the alternative view
The author notes that many mutakallimun affirm mental existence, and those who do not can take the subject as 'the known' (al-ma'lum). Reflect on how this preserves the unity of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قيل : قد يبحث مع نفي الوجود الذهني عن أحوال ما لا يعتبر وجوده كالنظر والدليل وما لا وجود كالمعدوم والحال قلنا ولواحق ولو سلم فنفي الذهني رأي البعض). أقول : اعترض في المواقف على كون موضوع الكلام هو الموجود من حيث هو بأنه قد يبحث عن أحوال ما لا يعتبر وجوده، وإن كان موجوداً كالنظر والدليل وعن أحوال ما لا وجود له أصلاً كالمعدوم والحال ولا يجوز أن يؤخذ الموجود أعم من الذهني والخارجي ليعم الكل لأن المتكلمين لا يقولون بالوجود الذهني، والجواب : إنا لا نسلم كون هذه المباحث من مسائل الكلام بل مباحث النظر والدليل من مباديه على ما قررنا (۱) وبحث المعدوم والحال من لواحق مسألة الوجود

توضيحاً للمقصود وتتميماً له بالتعرض لما يقابله لا يقال بحث إعادة المعدوم، واستحالة التسلسل ، ونفي الهيولي (١) وأمثال ذلك من المسائل قطعاً، لأنا نقول هي راجعة إلى أحوال الموجود بأنه هل يعاد بعد العدم؟ وهل يتسلسل إلى غير النهاية؟ وهل يتركب الجسم من الهيولي والصورة؟ ولو سلم أنها من المسائل فإنما يريد (٢) ما ذكرتم لو أريد بالموجود من حيث هو الموجود (۳) في الخارج بشرط اعتبار وجوده وليس كذلك. بل الموجود على الإطلاق ذهنياً كان أو خارجياً واجباً أو ممكناً جوهراً أو عرضاً إلى غير ذلك. فمباحث النظر والدليل من أحوال الوجود العيني وإن لم يعتبره. والبواقي من أحوال الوجود الذهني، وكثير من المتكلمين يقولون به على ما يصرح بذلك كلامهم، ومن لم يقل فعليه العدول إلى المعلوم .
He said: For if it is said: Discussion occurs, along with the denial of mental existence, about states of what whose existence is not considered, such as consideration (al-nazar) and proof (al-dalil), and what has no existence at all like the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and the state (al-hal). We say: and the subsidiaries (lawahiq). And if it is granted that the denial of mental existence is the opinion of some. I say: An objection was raised in al-Mawaqif concerning the subject of kalam being existent qua existent, that it discusses states of what whose existence is not considered, even if it is existent like consideration and proof, and states of what has no existence at all like the non-existent and the state. And it is not permissible to take existent as more general than mental and external to include all, because the mutakallimun do not affirm mental existence. The answer: We do not grant that these investigations are among the issues of kalam; rather, the investigations of consideration and proof are among its principles (mabadi), as we have established, and the investigation of the non-existent and the state are among the subsidiaries (lawahiq) of the issue of existence.

Clarifying the intent and completing it by addressing what is opposed: It is not said that the discussion of the restoration of the non-existent, the impossibility of infinite regress, the denial of hyle, and similar matters are indeed issues. For we say: They go back to states of the existent: whether it is restored after non-existence? Whether it regresses infinitely? Whether the body is composed of hyle and form? And if it is granted that they are issues, then what you [objectors] mentioned would only apply if by 'existent qua existent' is meant existent in the external world with the condition of considering its existence, but it is not so. Rather, [it means] existent in an absolute sense, whether mental or external, necessary or possible, substance or accident, etc. So the investigations of consideration and proof are among the states of external existence, even if their existence is not considered. And the rest are among the states of mental existence, and many mutakallimun affirm it, as their words testify, and whoever does not affirm must turn to the known (al-ma'lum).
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer defending the definition of kalam's subject as 'existent qua existent'. The objector argues that mutakallimun often discuss things without existence, like 'consideration' (nazar) and 'proof' (dalil), or non-existents like the non-existent (ma'dum) and state (hal). Since they deny mental existence, the subject cannot be 'existent'. The author answers by distinguishing between the principles (mabadi) of kalam and its actual issues: discussions of consideration and proof are principles, not issues. The non-existent and state are subsidiaries of the issue of existence. In a clarifying addition, he responds to examples like the restoration of the non-existent, infinite regress, and hyle: these all relate to states of the existent. The meaning of 'existent' is taken absolutely, including mental and external, so investigations fall under states of external or mental existence. Many mutakallimun affirm mental existence; those who do not must take the subject as 'the known' (al-ma'lum) instead.

Key Terms
نظر
دليل
معدوم
حال
الوجود الذهني
الوجود العيني
مبادئ
لواحق
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any discussion of non-existents invalidates the definition of kalam's subject as existent qua existent.
Correction: The author shows that such discussions are either part of the principles or subsidiaries, not issues that conflict with the subject, and that 'existent' includes mental existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how are discussions of 'consideration' (nazar) and 'proof' (dalil) treated in relation to the subject of kalam?
A) They are among the issues (masa'il) of kalam.
B) They are among the principles (mabadi) of kalam.
C) They are irrelevant to kalam.
D) They are part of logic.

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam as 'existent qua existent' is preserved by classifying discussions of non-existents as principles or subsidiaries, and by taking 'existent' absolutely to include mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often discuss theological topics with others. Are you sure that the terms you use (like 'existence', 'non-existence') are defined clearly and consistently? Could your disagreements stem from loose definitions rather than real differences? How will you commit to clarifying definitions before debating from now on?
?
The author distinguishes between principles and issues in a science. What are the principles of your own faith—the foundational axioms you accept without question? Have you ever challenged or examined those principles carefully? If not, why do you accept them?
?
When you encounter an objection to your beliefs, do you immediately dismiss it, or do you take the time to see if the objection actually hits the subject or only touches irrelevant details? How can you train yourself to respond like the author, with careful analysis rather than reflexive denial?

What Is Kalam?

Alternative Subject of Kalam: God's Essence or Together with Possibles?
Sanusi presents an alternative definition of kalam's subject with elaboration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative definition of kalam's subject as the Essence of God alone or together with possible things in their dependence on Him.

Core Idea
Kalam's ultimate subject is God, but it also studies creation in relation to Him.

In this passage, the author presents a definition of kalam that differs from the one previously given. Instead of restricting the subject to God's essence alone, he suggests it may include possible things insofar as they depend on God. This opens the door to discussing created beings, but always in relation to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first part of the passage where the alternative definition is given. Note the two options: subject is God's essence alone, or together with possible things in their dependence on Him.
The phrase 'من حيث استنادها إليه' is crucial—possible things are considered only in relation to God.
2
Identify the elaboration
After stating the definition, the author elaborates by describing kalam as investigating the Maker's positive and negative attributes, His acts, or the states of the Necessary and possible in the beginning and return. See how the elaboration stays focused on God.
The terms 'المبدأ والمعاد' refer to the beginning of creation and the return to God.
3
Reflect on the inclusiveness
Consider why the alternative definition includes possible things. How does this broaden the scope of kalam while keeping God as the central subject?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقيل موضوعه ذات الله تعالى وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من حيث استنادها إليه لما أنه يبحث عن ذلك ولهذا يعرف بالعلم الباحث عن أحوال الصانع من صفاته الثبوتية والسلبية، وأفعاله المتعلقة بأمر الدنيا والآخرة أو عن أحوال الواجب، وأحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد على قانون الإسلام. فإن قيل : قد يبحث في الأمور العامة والجواهر والأعراض عن أحوال الممكنات لا على وجه الاستنا تلنا على سبيل الاستطراد للتكميل أو الحكاية للتزييف أو المبدأية من التحقيق ، وإلا فهو من فضول الكلام. فإن قيل : مبادئه يجب أن تكون بينة بنفسها إذ ليس فوقه علم شرعي.
He said: And it is said that its subject is the Essence of God, may He be exalted, alone, or together with the essences of possible things in respect of their dependence upon Him, because it investigates that. And for this reason it is defined as the science that investigates the states of the Maker—His positive and negative attributes, and His acts pertaining to the matters of this world and the hereafter—or the states of the Necessary Being and the states of possible things in the beginning and the return, according to the law of Islam. For if it is said: It investigates in the universal matters (al-umur al-'amma), substances, and accidents about the states of possible things, not in the manner of ... [perhaps 'dependency'] but by way of digression (istitrad) for completion, or by way of narration for refutation, or by way of principiality (mabda'iyya) for verification; otherwise it is superfluous talk. For if it is said: Its principles must be self-evident, since there is no revealed science above it.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage first presents an alternative definition of kalam's subject: the Essence of God alone, or together with possible things in their dependence on Him. The definition is elaborated as investigating the Maker's attributes and acts, or the Necessary and the possible in the beginning and return (al-mabda' wa'l-ma'ad). Then two objections are raised. The first objection says that kalam also investigates universal matters, substances, and accidents, which are about possible things, not directly about God. The answer: these are investigated not as the main subject but by way of digression for completion, narration for refutation, or principle for verification; otherwise they would be extraneous. The second objection (incomplete in the window) says that the principles of kalam must be self-evident because there is no revealed science above it to provide evidence. The answer to this objection is not given in the available text.

Key Terms
موضوع الكلام
ذات الله
الصانع
صفات ثبوتية وسلبية
المبدأ والمعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think kalam only discusses God directly and never engages with created things.
Correction: This passage shows that kalam can discuss possible things, but only in their dependence on God and always in relation to Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two options for the subject of kalam?
A) Only the Essence of God
B) The Essence of God alone or together with possible things in their dependence on Him
C) The Essence of God and the essences of all possible things absolutely
D) Only the possible things

Key Takeaway
Kalam's subject is fundamentally God, and any discussion of creation is always in reference to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you truly see yourself as a 'possible thing' that is completely dependent on God at every moment? How would your daily anxieties change if you lived this conviction fully?
?
The lesson says kalam investigates God's acts 'pertaining to the matters of this world and the hereafter'. Which act of God in your life right now are you failing to recognize as His? What would it mean to trace it back to Him?
?
If the ultimate subject of kalam is God, then even your deepest intellectual questions about existence must be framed around Him. Are you willing to let go of studying creation independently and instead see everything only in relation to your Creator? What step will you take today to shift that focus?

What Is Kalam?

Responding to the First Objection: Digression vs. Main Subject
Sanusi raises and answers an objection about kalam discussing universal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends kalam's subject by explaining that discussions of universal matters, substances, and accidents are incidental, not central.

Core Idea
Discussions of general metaphysics in kalam are by way of digression, not as the main subject.

After defining kalam's subject, the author anticipates an objection: if kalam investigates universal matters, substances, and accidents, doesn't it stray from discussing God? The answer given is that these topics are included only as digressions for specific purposes, not as the primary focus. This clarifies the boundaries of kalam's subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection starting with 'فإن قيل: قد يبحث في الأمور العامة...' Identify what the objector claims: that kalam discusses universal matters, substances, and accidents, which are about possible things, not God.
The objector is pointing out that kalam seems to cover general metaphysics, which would contradict the definition that subject is God.
2
Identify the answer's structure
The author responds by saying these topics are not investigated as the main subject, but in three specific ways. Find those three ways and understand each.
3
Understand the three purposes
The three purposes are: (1) digression (istitrad) for completion, (2) narration (hikaya) for refutation, (3) principiality (mabda'iyya) for verification. Consider examples for each.
'Completion' means elaborating on something related; 'refutation' means discussing false views; 'verification' means establishing principles.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The author concludes that without these purposes, such discussions would be superfluous talk (fudul al-kalam). This reinforces that kalam's core subject remains God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقيل موضوعه ذات الله تعالى وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من حيث استنادها إليه لما أنه يبحث عن ذلك ولهذا يعرف بالعلم الباحث عن أحوال الصانع من صفاته الثبوتية والسلبية، وأفعاله المتعلقة بأمر الدنيا والآخرة أو عن أحوال الواجب، وأحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد على قانون الإسلام. فإن قيل : قد يبحث في الأمور العامة والجواهر والأعراض عن أحوال الممكنات لا على وجه الاستنا تلنا على سبيل الاستطراد للتكميل أو الحكاية للتزييف أو المبدأية من التحقيق ، وإلا فهو من فضول الكلام. فإن قيل : مبادئه يجب أن تكون بينة بنفسها إذ ليس فوقه علم شرعي.
He said: And it is said that its subject is the Essence of God, may He be exalted, alone, or together with the essences of possible things in respect of their dependence upon Him, because it investigates that. And for this reason it is defined as the science that investigates the states of the Maker—His positive and negative attributes, and His acts pertaining to the matters of this world and the hereafter—or the states of the Necessary Being and the states of possible things in the beginning and the return, according to the law of Islam. For if it is said: It investigates in the universal matters (al-umur al-'amma), substances, and accidents about the states of possible things, not in the manner of ... [perhaps 'dependency'] but by way of digression (istitrad) for completion, or by way of narration for refutation, or by way of principiality (mabda'iyya) for verification; otherwise it is superfluous talk. For if it is said: Its principles must be self-evident, since there is no revealed science above it.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage first presents an alternative definition of kalam's subject: the Essence of God alone, or together with possible things in their dependence on Him. The definition is elaborated as investigating the Maker's attributes and acts, or the Necessary and the possible in the beginning and return (al-mabda' wa'l-ma'ad). Then two objections are raised. The first objection says that kalam also investigates universal matters, substances, and accidents, which are about possible things, not directly about God. The answer: these are investigated not as the main subject but by way of digression for completion, narration for refutation, or principle for verification; otherwise they would be extraneous. The second objection (incomplete in the window) says that the principles of kalam must be self-evident because there is no revealed science above it to provide evidence. The answer to this objection is not given in the available text.

Key Terms

الاستطراد
المبادئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because kalam discusses general metaphysics, its subject is not God but all of reality.
Correction: The author clarifies that such discussions are only incidental and serve specific purposes, not as the main subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what basis does kalam discuss universal matters, substances, and accidents?
A) They are the main subject of kalam.
B) They are discussed as part of the subject of the Maker.
C) They are discussed only by way of digression, narration, or principiality.
D) They are discussed because they are self-evident principles.

Key Takeaway
Kalam's discussions of general metaphysical topics are incidental (istitrad, hikaya, mabda'iyya), not central to its subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, are you tempted to get sidetracked by fascinating metaphysical discussions, forgetting that the ultimate purpose is to know and worship Allah? How can you ensure that your study of these topics remains a means to draw closer to Him, rather than an intellectual distraction?
?
Have you ever prioritized debating abstract theological concepts over acting on what you already know about Allah and His commands? What would it take for you to shift your focus back to the heart of servitude?
?
If you were to examine your own religious studies, is there any area where you are pursuing knowledge for its own sake rather than as a tool for taqwa and action? What concrete step can you take today to realign your learning with your ultimate goal?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
Refuting the Objection: All Kalam Topics Relate to the Existent
Author refutes objection by distinguishing absolute existent from external existent and including mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the definition of kalam's subject as the existent by showing that seemingly unrelated topics are actually about states of the existent, and by clarifying that 'existent' includes mental existence.

Core Idea
Every topic in kalam is ultimately about the existent — external or mental.

The author has defined the subject of kalam as the existent. But an objector might point to topics like the re-creation of the non-existent or infinite regress and claim they are not about the existent. This lesson shows how the author refutes that objection by returning these topics to states of the existent and by broadening the meaning of 'existent' to include both external and mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection, the response, the clarification of terms, and the alternative solution.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Find where the author states the objection (about re-creation, infinite regress, hyle) and how he responds by arguing these topics revert to states of the existent.
The author says 'we say: they revert to states of the existent'.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between the existent as external (conditioned on existence) and the existent absolutely (including mental existence). Understand why this distinction is crucial for the refutation.
The objector's criticism only holds if 'existent' means only external existent.
4
Consider the alternative: 'the known'
Learn that some theologians resort to using 'the known' as the subject of kalam instead of the existent. This is presented as a fallback position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توضيحاً للمقصود وتتميماً له بالتعرض لما يقابله لا يقال بحث إعادة المعدوم، واستحالة التسلسل ، ونفي الهيولي وأمثال ذلك من المسائل قطعاً، لأنا نقول هي راجعة إلى أحوال الموجود بأنه هل يعاد بعد العدم؟ وهل يتسلسل إلى غير النهاية؟ وهل يتركب الجسم من الهيولي والصورة؟ ولو سلم أنها من المسائل فإنما يريد ما ذكرتم لو أريد بالموجود من حيث هو الموجود في الخارج بشرط اعتبار وجوده وليس كذلك. بل الموجود على الإطلاق ذهنياً كان أو خارجياً واجباً أو ممكناً جوهراً أو عرضاً إلى غير ذلك فمباحث النظر والدليل من أحوال الوجود العيني وإن لم يعتبره. والبواقي من أحوال الوجود الذهني، وكثير من المتكلمين يقولون به على ما يصرح بذلك كلامهم، ومن لم يقل فعليه العدول إلى المعلوم .
Clarification of the intended meaning and completion of it by addressing what corresponds to it. It should not be said: the investigation of the re-creation of the non-existent, the impossibility of infinite regress, the denial of hyle, and similar matters are [not] topics at all, because we say: they revert to states of the existent: whether it is re-created after non-existence? Whether it regresses infinitely? Whether the body is composed of hyle and form? And if it is granted that they are topics, then [the objection] would only be as you mentioned if by 'the existent' were meant the existent qua existent in the external world with the condition of considering its existence, but it is not so. Rather, the existent absolutely—whether mental or external, necessary or possible, substance or accident, and so forth. So the investigations of theory and proof are from the states of external existence even if he did not consider it. And the remainder are from the states of mental existence. Many theologians affirm this, as their statements make clear, and whoever does not say so must turn to the known.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the definition of the subject of kalam as the existent. The author anticipates an objection: topics like re-creation of the non-existent, impossibility of infinite regress, and denial of hyle (prime matter) are not about the existent. He refutes this by arguing that these topics investigate states of the existent: whether it is re-created after non-existence, whether it regresses infinitely, whether body is composed of hyle and form. Even if they were separate topics, the objection would hold only if 'existent' meant a particular existent in the external world conditioned on existence. But the intended meaning is the absolute existent, encompassing mental and external, necessary and possible, substance and accident. Thus, investigations of theory and proof deal with states of external existence (even if not explicitly considered by the objector), and the remaining topics concern mental existence. Many theologians adopt this view; if one does not, they must shift the subject to the 'known' instead.

Key Terms
الموجود
إعادة المعدوم
التسلسل
الهيولي
الوجود الذهني
الوجود العيني
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Topics like re-creation of the non-existent and infinite regress are not about the existent at all.
Correction: These topics are about states of the existent, because 'the existent' includes both external and mental existence, and these topics investigate the conditions and possibilities of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why does the objection regarding topics like re-creation of the non-existent not invalidate the definition of kalam's subject as the existent?
A) Because those topics are not actually discussed in kalam.
B) Because the existent is meant absolutely, including mental existence, and these topics are about states of the existent.
C) Because the objector is mistaken about what kalam discusses.
D) Because those topics are about the known rather than the existent.

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam, the existent, is understood absolutely to include both external and mental existence, thus encompassing all topics discussed in theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your intellectual beliefs (like about infinite regress or the nature of the soul) as merely abstract concepts, or do you acknowledge them as real existents in your mind that affect your relationship with Allah? This lesson teaches that mental existence is part of the subject of theology. How does that challenge your tendency to separate 'theory' from 'practice' in your faith? What will you do today to take one theoretical Islamic belief and treat it as a real existence in your heart?
?
The author insists that topics like hyle and infinite regress are about the existent. Are there any theological topics you have dismissed as irrelevant because they seem purely theoretical? How does this lesson expose your neglect of the full breadth of Islamic knowledge? Commit to studying one such topic this week.
?
Many theologians affirm mental existence as real. Do you give the same weight to your inner thoughts, intentions, and beliefs as you do to your outward actions? This lesson suggests both are existents that Allah holds you accountable for. What hidden belief or mental habit are you avoiding confronting? Write it down and ask Allah for forgiveness.

The Subject of Kalam: Allah's Essence and Possible Beings
He says that the subject is Allah's essence or with possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the subject of 'ilm al-kalam according to some scholars, and how it relates to Allah's essence and possible beings.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is Allah's essence, or includes possible beings in relation to Him.

This lesson presents an alternative definition of the subject of kalam. Some scholars hold that the subject is Allah's essence alone, or together with possible beings in their dependence on Him. This definition shapes what kalam investigates and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the subject of kalam
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two opinions presented.
The passage starts with 'قال : وقيل موضوعه'.
2
Identify the two opinions on the subject
The passage mentions two views: the subject is Allah's essence alone, or the essence along with possible beings in their relation to Him. Understand the difference.
3
Understand the resulting definition of kalam
The passage then defines kalam as the science investigating the Maker's attributes and acts, or the states of the Necessary Being and possible beings in the beginning and return. This definition follows from the subject.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقيل موضوعه ذات الله تعالى وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من حيث استنادها إليه لما أنه يبحث عن ذلك ولهذا يعرف بالعلم الباحث عن أحوال الصانع من صفاته الثبوتية والسلبية، وأفعاله المتعلقة بأمر الدنيا والآخرة أو عن أحوال الواجب، وأحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد على قانون الإسلام. فإن قيل : قد يبحث في الأمور العامة والجواهر والأعراض عن أحوال الممكنات لا على وجه الاستطراد للتكميل أو الحكاية للتزييف أو المبدأية من التحقيق، وإلا فهو من فضول الكلام.
He said: And it is said: Its subject is the essence of God Most High alone, or along with the essences of possible beings in relation to their dependence on Him, because it investigates that. Hence it is defined as the science that investigates the states of the Maker—His positive and negative attributes, and His acts related to the affairs of this world and the Hereafter—or the states of the Necessary Being, and the states of possible beings in the beginning and the return, according to the law of Islam. For it is said: One sometimes investigates in the general matters, substances, and accidents the states of possible beings not by way of digression for completion, or as a report for refutation, or as a preliminary to the investigation; otherwise it would be idle talk.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of an alternate definition of the subject of kalam, followed by an objection and answer. The author reports that some say the subject is either the essence of God alone or together with the essences of possible beings in their relation to Him, because kalam investigates that. Consequently, it is defined as the science investigating the states of the Maker—His positive and negative attributes, and His acts pertaining to this world (e.g., creation, prophethood) and the Hereafter (e.g., resurrection)—or the states of the Necessary Being and possible beings in the beginning and the end, according to Islamic law. Then an objection is raised: sometimes kalam investigates general matters, substances and accidents, which are about possible beings without such a relation. The answer: such investigations are either by way of digression (istitrad) for completion, or as a report for refutation (hikaya li al-tazyif), or as a preliminary (mabda'iyya) to the investigation. Otherwise, they would be superfluous speech (fudul al-kalam). Thus, not every investigation of possible beings is part of the subject of kalam.

Addressing the Objection: How Kalam Investigates Possible Beings
An objection is raised about investigating general matters, and the author answers with three valid reasons.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Investigations of possible beings without direct relation to Allah are justified as digression, refutation, or preliminary.

An objection is raised: isn't it true that kalam often discusses general matters, substances, and accidents, which are about possible beings without explicit relation to Allah? The author provides an answer explaining that such discussions fall under specific categories—digression for completion, report for refutation, or as a preliminary—and would otherwise be superfluous.

3

The Subject of Kalam: Two Scholarly Views
Author relates al-Urmawi's view and al-Samarqandi's addition regarding the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Urmawi and al-Samarqandi defined the subject of kalam and what addition al-Samarqandi made.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is Allah's essence and its relation to possible beings.

In this passage, the author presents two influential views on the subject matter of kalam. Al-Urmawi limits it to the essence of God, while al-Samarqandi expands it to include the relationship between God and possible beings. Understanding these definitions clarifies the scope of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Pay attention to the technical terms used.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the complete opinions presented.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where al-Urmawi's definition is stated and where al-Samarqandi's addition begins. Note the key difference.
Al-Urmawi focuses on the Maker's attributes and acts; al-Samarqandi adds the states of possible beings.
3
Understand the unifying concept
Al-Samarqandi introduces 'the existent' (al-mawjud) as what unifies the subject. Reflect on why this matters for the science of kalam.
This expansion aligns with kalam's coverage of both divine and creaturely matters.

I say: Al-Qadi al-Urmawi among the later scholars held that the subject of kalam is the essence of God Most High, because it investigates His positive and negative attributes, and His acts related to the affairs of this world, like the manner of the world's emanation from Him by choice, the origination of the world, the creation of actions, the system of the world, such as the investigation of prophethood and what follows, or related to the Hereafter like the investigation of the resurrection and other sam'iyyat. Thus kalam is the science investigating the states of the Maker—His positive and negative attributes and His acts related to the affairs of this world and the Hereafter. The author of al-Saha'if followed him, but added that he made the subject the essence of God Most High as such, and the essence [of possible beings] in its relation to God Most High, because it investigates essential descriptions of the essence of God Most High as such, and essential descriptions of the essence of possible beings insofar as they are in need of God Most High. The unifying aspect is the existent. So it is the science investigating the states of the Maker and the states of possible beings in their need of Him, according to the law of Islam. This should be the meaning of what he said: 'It is the science investigating the essence of God Most High, His attributes, and the states of possible beings in the beginning and the return, according to the law of Islam.' Otherwise, there is no meaning to investigating the subject itself. But he answered that the materials in the definition are the essence in terms of attributes, like the essence in terms of non-compositeness, substantiality, and accidentality. Investigating them is of the category of problems, like investigating the attributes themselves; the essence as such is not investigated in the science. And this indicates that the predicate in
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of two related opinions on the subject matter of kalam. The author begins by stating that al-Qadi al-Urmawi held that the subject is the essence of God because kalam investigates His positive and negative attributes and His acts related to this world (e.g., emanation of the world by choice, origination of the world, creation of actions, prophethood) and the Hereafter (e.g., resurrection and sam'iyyat). Thus kalam is the science of the Maker's states. The author of al-Saha'if (al-Samarqandi) followed al-Urmawi but expanded the subject to include both the essence of God as such and the essences of possible beings in their relation to God, since kalam investigates essential descriptions of God's essence as such and essential descriptions of possible beings as needing God. The unifying principle is 'the existent' (al-mawjud). He says this should be the meaning of the phrase 'the science investigating the essence of God, His attributes, and the states of possible beings in the beginning and the return.' Otherwise, investigating the subject itself would be pointless. He answers that the 'materials' (al-mawad) in the definition are the essence in terms of attributes—like the essence in terms of non-compositeness, substantiality, and accidentality—and investigating them is a type of problem (masa'il), like investigating the attributes themselves; the essence as such is not investigated in the science. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'this indicates that the predicate in...'

Al-Samarqandi expanded the subject of kalam from God's essence alone to include possible beings in their relation to God, with 'the existent' as the unifying principle.

Views on the Subject of Kalam: Al-Armaawi and Al-Samaraqandi
Al-Taftazani presents the views of al-Armaawi and al-Samaraqandi and clarifies the relationship between subject and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of the subject of kalam and how investigating attributes relates to the subject's definition.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is not just God's essence; it also encompasses all things in relation to Him.

What is the subject matter of kalam? Two later scholars offered contrasting definitions. Qadi al-Armaawi narrowed it to God's essence alone, while the author of al-Sahafif expanded it to include the essences of possible things in their dependence on God. A crucial clarification shows that investigating attributes does not change the subject but belongs to the problems of the science. This lesson covers both views and the clarification that defends al-Samaraqandi's definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two scholars
The passage first presents Qadi al-Armaawi's view, then the author of al-Sahafif's addition. Note how each defines the subject.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each view
Al-Armaawi argues that because kalam investigates God's attributes and acts, its subject is God's essence. Al-Samaraqandi adds that it also investigates possibles insofar as they relate to God, so their essences in that relation are also subjects.
The unifying aspect is existence (al-mawjud).
3
Examine the clarification about attributes
The passage explains that investigating attributes is not investigating the subject itself; it is investigating the subject under a description. This shows that the predicate 'not a substance' applies to the essence under the description of lacking substantiality.
This clarifies that attributes are problems (masa'il), not part of the subject.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

معنى ما :قال هو العلم الباحث عن ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وأحوال الممكنات في المبدأ والمعاد على قانون الإسلام، وإلا فلا معنى للبحث عن نفس الموضوع، لكنه أجاب بأن المواد بذات الله تعالى في التعريف الذات من بجوهر (۱) ولا (عرض) (۲) هو ذات الله تعالى من حيث عدم الجوهرية والعرضية . فإن قيل : لو كان الموضوع ذات الله تعالى وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من حيث الاستناد إليه لما وقع البحث في المسائل إلا عن أحوالها، واللازم باطل، لأن كثيراً من مباحث الأمور العامة والجواهر والأعراض بحث عن أحوال الممكنات لا من حيث استنادها إلى الواجب . ، قلنا: يجوز أن يكون ذلك على سبيل الاستطراد، قصداً إلى تكميل الصناعة، بأن يذكر مع المطلوب ماله نوع تعلق من اللواحق والفروع والمقابلات، وما أشبه ذلك، كمباحث المعدوم والحال وأقسام الماهية والحركات والأجسام، أو على سبيل الحكاية لكلام المخالف، قصداً إلى تزييفه كبحث علة اليقين (۳) والآثار العلوية والجواهر المجردة، أو على سبيل المبدئية بأن يتوقف عليه بعض المسائل فيذكر لتحقيق المقصود بأن لا يتوقف بيانه على ما ليس يبين (4) كاشتراك الوجود واستحالة التسلسل، وجواز كون الشيء قابلاً وفاعلا ، وإمكان الخلاء وتناهي الأبعاد، وأما ما سوى ذلك فيكون من فضول الكلام،
Possibles. I say: Qadi al-Armaawi (1) among the later scholars held that the subject of kalam is the essence of God Almighty (dhat Allah ta'ala), because it investigates His positive and negative attributes (sifat thubutiyya wa-salbiyya), and His acts pertaining to the affairs of this world, such as how the world emanates from Him by choice, the origination of the world, the creation of actions, and the order of the world, like the investigation of prophecies and what follows them, or pertaining to the affairs of the Hereafter, like the investigation of the resurrection and all sam'iyyat. Thus kalam is the science that investigates the states of the Maker, His positive and negative attributes, and His acts pertaining to this world and the Hereafter. He was followed by the author of al-Sahafif (2), except that he added and made the subject [both] the essence of God Almighty in itself, and the essence [of possibles] in terms of their dependence on God Almighty (3), because he investigates essential attributes of the essence of God Almighty in itself, and essential attributes of the essences of possibles (mumkinat) insofar as they are in need of God Almighty. The unifying aspect is existence (al-mawjud). And he [al-Katibi] considered it the science that investigates the states of the Maker and the states of possibles in terms of their need for Him according to the law of Islam. This should be the meaning of what he said: 'It is the science that investigates the essence of God Almighty and His attributes and the states of possibles in the beginning and the return according to the law of Islam.' Otherwise, there is no meaning to investigating the subject itself. But he responded that the matters are about the essence of God Almighty in the definition: the essence in terms of attributes is like the essence in terms of non-composition and substantiality and accidentality, and investigating them is of the type of problems (masa'il), like investigating the attributes themselves; the essence in itself is not investigated in the science. And this indicates (4) that the predicate in our statement 'the Necessary is not a substance (1) nor an accident (2)' is the essence of God Almighty in terms of the absence of substantiality and accidentality. Then it was said: If the subject were the essence of God Almighty alone, or together with the essences of possibles in terms of dependence on Him, then the investigation in the problems would only concern their states, but the concomitant is false, because many investigations of universal matters, substances, and accidents investigate the states of possibles not in terms of their dependence on the Necessary. We say: That may be by way of digression (istitrad), aiming at perfecting the craft, by mentioning along with the sought thing what has a type of connection from among concomitants, branches, opposites, and the like, such as the investigations of non-existent (ma'dum), state (hal), divisions of quiddity (mahiyya), motions, and bodies; or by way of reporting the speech of an opponent intending to refute it, like the investigation of the cause of certainty, the celestial effects, and the incorporeal substances; or by way of being a principle (mabda'iyya), because some problems depend on it, so it is mentioned to realize the intended purpose, without its explanation depending on what is not clarified, like the community of existence (ishtirak al-wujud), the impossibility of regress, the possibility of a thing being both receptive and agent, the possibility of void, and the finitude of dimensions. As for what is beyond that, it is superfluous talk.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute over the subject matter of kalam and addresses an objection. It begins by stating that Qadi al-Armaawi held that the subject is the essence of God Almighty alone, because kalam investigates God's positive and negative attributes and His acts in this world and the Hereafter. Then the author of al-Sahafif (al-Samaraqandi) is mentioned, who added that the subject includes also the essences of possibles insofar as they depend on God, with existence as the unifying principle. The passage then explains that al-Samaraqandi's definition means investigating God's essence, attributes, and the states of possibles in the beginning and return (al-mabda' wa al-ma'ad) according to Islamic law. A clarification is given that investigating attributes is not investigating the subject itself, but rather problems (masa'il). The objection states that if the subject were limited to God or God plus possibles, then all problems would only concern their states, but many kalam topics (like universal matters, substances, accidents) are about possibles without reference to God. The answer classifies such topics into three categories: 1) digression (istitrad) to perfect the science, like discussions of non-existent, state, etc.; 2) reporting and refuting opponents' views, like the cause of certainty; 3) serving as principles for other problems, like community of existence, impossibility of regress, etc. Everything else is superfluous.

Key Terms

ذات الله تعالى
صفات ثبوتية وسلبية
الممكنات

A) Existence
B) Essence
C) Attributes
D) Dependence

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam, according to al-Samaraqandi, includes both God's essence and the essences of possible things in their relation to God, unified by existence.

Objection and Response: Expanding the Scope of Kalam Problems
Al-Taftazani presents an objection to the subject definitions and responds by categorizing apparently unrelated topics into three classes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that many kalam topics seem unrelated to God and the three methodological justifications for their inclusion.

Core Idea
Kalam legitimately includes discussions of possibles as digressions, refutations of opponents, or necessary principles.

An objector argues that if kalam's subject is limited to God or God-plus-possibles-in-dependence, then all problems should be about their states. Yet many kalam topics—like universal matters, substances, and accidents—appear to be about possible things without any direct reference to God. Al-Taftazani provides a threefold answer, classifying such topics as either digression (istitrad), refutation (hikaya), or principle (mabda'iyya). This lesson presents the objection and its sophisticated response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that if the subject is as defined, then problems should only concern the states of the subject, but in fact many problems are about possibles without reference to God. Understand the logical structure.
The objector thinks this refutes the definitions.
2
Identify the three categories in the answer
Al-Taftazani gives three justifications: digression (to perfect the science), reporting and refuting opponents, and being a principle for other problems. Note examples for each.
Digression includes topics like non-existent, state, divisions of quiddity; refutation includes cause of certainty; principles include community of existence, impossibility of regress.
3
Understand the implication of the third category
Some problems are included because they are necessary preliminaries for the main subject. They do not form part of the subject but are needed to establish it.
4
Note the final statement
Everything beyond these three categories is considered superfluous talk, indicating a disciplined approach to the scope of kalam.

بجوهر (۱) ولا (عرض) (۲) هو ذات الله تعالى من حيث عدم الجوهرية والعرضية . فإن قيل : لو كان الموضوع ذات الله تعالى وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من حيث الاستناد إليه لما وقع البحث في المسائل إلا عن أحوالها، واللازم باطل، لأن كثيراً من مباحث الأمور العامة والجواهر والأعراض بحث عن أحوال الممكنات لا من حيث استنادها إلى الواجب . ، قلنا: يجوز أن يكون ذلك على سبيل الاستطراد، قصداً إلى تكميل الصناعة، بأن يذكر Possibles. I say: Qadi al-Armaawi (1) among the later scholars held that the subject of kalam is the essence of God Almighty (dhat Allah ta'ala), because it investigates His positive and negative attributes (sifat thubutiyya wa-salbiyya), and His acts pertaining to the affairs of this world, such as how the world emanates from Him by choice, the origination of the world, the creation of actions, and the order of the world, like the investigation of prophecies and what follows them, or pertaining to the affairs of the Hereafter, like the investigation of the resurrection and all sam'iyyat. Thus kalam is the science that investigates the states of the Maker, His positive and negative attributes, and His acts pertaining to this world and the Hereafter. He was followed by the author of al-Sahafif (2), except that he added and made the subject [both] the essence of God Almighty in itself, and the essence [of possibles] in terms of their dependence on God Almighty (3), because he investigates essential attributes of the essence of God Almighty in itself, and essential attributes of the essences of possibles (mumkinat) insofar as they are in need of God Almighty. The unifying aspect is existence (al-mawjud). And he [al-Katibi] considered it the science that investigates the states of the Maker and the states of possibles in terms of their need for Him according to the law of Islam. This should be the meaning of what he said: 'It is the science that investigates the essence of God Almighty and His attributes and the states of possibles in the beginning and the return according to the law of Islam.' Otherwise, there is no meaning to investigating the subject itself. But he responded that the matters are about the essence of God Almighty in the definition: the essence in terms of attributes is like the essence in terms of non-composition and substantiality and accidentality, and investigating them is of the type of problems (masa'il), like investigating the attributes themselves; the essence in itself is not investigated in the science. As for what is beyond that, it is superfluous talk.

The Subject of Kalam and Justification of Digressions
Defines the subject of kalam and answers an objection about including unrelated topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Kalam includes topics that do not directly discuss God's essence, and the three legitimate justifications for such digressions.

Core Idea
Digressions in Kalam are not random; they serve to complete the discipline, refute opponents, or provide foundational principles.

The passage begins by defining the subject of Kalam as God's essence considered negatively (neither substance nor accident). It then raises a natural objection: if the subject is so narrow, why do Kalam texts discuss universals, substances, accidents, and other topics that seem unrelated? The author responds with a nuanced defense, showing that such discussions fall under three permissible categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection that if the subject is only God's essence, then many Kalam topics would be irrelevant.
This sets up the need for justification.
3
Study the three justifications
Identify each justification: istitrād, ḥikāya, and mabdaʾiyya. Note the examples given for each.
These are the author's main points.
4
Understand the limit
Note that anything beyond these three is considered superfluous (fuḍūl al-kalām), and the author criticizes later scholars for mixing physics and mathematics.
This defines the boundary of the discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا الواجب ليس بجوهر ولا عرض هو ذات الله تعالى من حيث عدم الجوهرية والعرضية. فإن قيل: لو كان الموضوع ذات الله تعالى وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من حيث الاستناد إليه لما وقع البحث في المسائل إلا عن أحوالها، واللازم باطل، لأن كثيراً من مباحث الأمور العامة والجواهر والأعراض بحث عن أحوال الممكنات لا من حيث استنادها إلى الواجب. قلنا: يجوز أن يكون ذلك على سبيل الاستطراد، قصداً إلى تكميل الصناعة، بأن يذكر مع المطلوب ماله نوع تعلق من اللواحق والفروع والمقابلات، وما أشبه ذلك، كمباحث المعدوم والحال وأقسام الماهية والحركات والأجسام، أو على سبيل الحكاية لكلام المخالف، قصداً إلى تزييفه كبحث علة اليقين والآثار العلوية والجواهر المجردة، أو على سبيل المبدئية بأن يتوقف عليه بعض المسائل فيذكر لتحقيق المقصود بأن لا يتوقف بيانه على ما ليس ببين كاشتراك الوجود واستحالة التسلسل، وجواز كون الشيء قابلاً وفاعلا، وإمكان الخلاء وتناهي الأبعاد، وأما ما سوى ذلك فيكون من فضول الكلام، يقصد به تكثير المباحث كما اشتهر فيما بين المتأخرين من خلط كثير من مسائل الطبيعي والرياضي بالكلام.
Our statement: 'The Necessary is not a substance (jawhar) nor an accident ('arad)' means the essence of God Most High considered from the aspect of not being substantial or accidental. If it is said: If the subject were only the essence of God Most High alone, or together with the essences of possible things considered from the aspect of their dependency upon Him, then inquiry in the [various] topics would only be about their states, but the necessary consequence is false, because many of the investigations of universal matters (al-umūr al-ʿāmma) and [those of] substances and accidents inquire into the states of possible things not from the aspect of their dependency upon the Necessary. We say: It is permissible that this be by way of digression (istiṭrād), aiming at completing the discipline (ṣināʿa), by mentioning along with what is intended whatever has some connection of accessories, branches, opposites, and the like — such as the investigations of the non-existent (maʿdūm), the state (ḥāl), the divisions of quiddity (māhiyya), motions, and bodies; or by way of reporting (ḥikāya) the speech of an opponent, aiming at its refutation — such as the investigation of the cause of certainty, the celestial effects, and the separate substances (al-jawāhir al-mujarrada); or by way of being a principle (mabdaʾiyya), in that some topics depend on it, so it is mentioned for the realization of the intended conclusion, such that its clarification does not depend on what is not evident — such as the commonality of existence, the impossibility of infinite regress, the permissibility of a thing being both recipient and agent, the possibility of void, and the finitude of dimensions. As for what is beyond that, it is superfluous speech (fuḍūl al-kalām) intended to multiply investigations, as is well-known among the later scholars with regard to the mixing of many of the topics of physics and mathematics into speculative theology (kalām).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of Kalam and defends the inclusion of topics that do not directly relate to God's essence. The author begins by stating that the Necessary Being (al-wājib) is neither a substance nor an accident, and that the subject of Kalam is God's essence considered from that negative aspect. An objection is then raised: if the subject is only God's essence (or includes possible beings only in their dependency on Him), then many Kalam discussions—like those on universals, substances, accidents—would be irrelevant because they treat possible beings without reference to God. The author answers by offering three legitimate justifications for such discussions: (1) istitrād (digression) to complete the discipline by including related topics; (2) ḥikāya (reporting) of the opponent's view in order to refute it; (3) mabdaʾiyya (being a principle) because some topics depend on these discussions as foundational premises. He gives examples for each, such as the investigation of the non-existent (maʿdūm) for digression, the cause of certainty for reporting, and the commonality of existence for principle. Anything beyond these three categories is considered superfluous (fuḍūl al-kalām), and the author criticizes later scholars for mixing physics and mathematics into Kalam unnecessarily.

واجب
جوهر
عرض
موضوع
إستطراد
حكاية
مبدئية
فضول الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Kalam discussions must directly address God's attributes or essence.
Correction: While the subject of Kalam is God's essence, the discipline legitimately includes digressions that complete the discipline, refute opponents, or provide foundational principles.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three justifications the author gives for including topics not directly about God in Kalam?
A) Istitrād (digression) to complete the discipline
B) Ḥikāya (reporting) to refute opponents
C) Taqlīd (imitation) of earlier scholars
D) Mabdaʾiyya (being a principle) for other topics

Key Takeaway
Kalam is not narrowly confined to God's essence; it legitimately includes related discussions as long as they serve one of three purposes: discipline completion, opponent refutation, or foundational principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own pursuit of knowledge, how often do you find yourself straying into topics that are interesting but not directly related to your goal? Are these digressions beneficial, or are they 'fuḍūl al-kalām' that merely multiply investigations without fulfilling a clear purpose? Pause and list the three legitimate categories from this lesson. Evaluate one of your recent study sessions against them.
?
When you encounter an opposing viewpoint, do you truly listen to it with the intention of refuting it (ḥikāya), or do you instinctively dismiss or ridicule it? This lesson teaches that reporting the opponent's speech is a legitimate part of completing the discipline. Will you commit to understanding the strongest version of an opposing argument before you respond?
?
The author warns against mixing physics and mathematics into kalām beyond what is necessary. Where in your life do you mix things that are not meant to be mixed—perhaps mixing secular pursuits with spiritual ones, or letting worldly concerns dilute your focus on Allah? Will you examine your priorities today and cut out the superfluous 'fuḍūl' that multiplies your busyness but not your closeness to Allah?

What Is Kalam?

The Subject of a Science and Its Application to Kalam
Taftazani states the principle and applies it to kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that the subject of a science is not proven within itself, and how this principle relates to the science of kalam, particularly regarding the proof of God's existence.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is proven in a higher science—kalam's highest demand is proving the Necessary Being.

This passage from Taftazani introduces a key epistemological principle: no science proves its own subject matter; that is done in a higher science, until we reach a self-evident subject. He then applies this to kalam, clarifying that proving God's existence is indeed a central task of kalam, contrary to some earlier scholars who thought otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle
The passage begins with a general epistemological rule: the subject of a science is not proven within that science, but in a higher science, until one reaches a subject that is self-evident (like 'the existent').
This is a foundational principle in classical Islamic classification of sciences.
2
Historical Application to Kalam
Early scholars applied this principle to kalam, arguing that if God were the subject of kalam, then proving His existence would have to occur in a higher science, not in kalam. Therefore, they concluded that proving God's existence is not part of kalam's subject matter.
Taftazani notes that the original scope of kalam is limited to discussions of Essence, Attributes, Prophethood, Imamate, and the Return.
3
Taftazani's Correction
Taftazani argues that there is no dispute that proving the Necessary Being is among the highest demands of kalam. He refutes the idea that this belongs to metaphysics instead, asserting that kalam is the chief of all Islamic sciences.
He emphasizes that the reality of a science is proving essential accidents of its subject, which presupposes the existence of that subject (simple proposition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وموضوع العلم لا يبين فيه، بل فيما فوقه حتى ينتهي إلى ما موضوعه بين الوجود كالموجود، من حيث هو أقول: لما كان من المباحث الحكمية ما لا يقدح في العقائد الدينية، ولم يناسب غير الكلام من العلوم الإسلامية، خلطها المتأخرون بمسائل الكلام إفاضة للحقائق، وإفادة لما أن يستعان به في التقصي عن المضايق عسى وإلا فلا نزاع في أن أصل الكلام لا يتجاوز مباحث الذات والصفات والنبوة والإمامة والمعاد، وما يتعلق بذلك من أحوال الممكنات، فلذا اقتصر القوم في إبطال كون موضوع الكلام ذات الله وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من جهة الاستناد على أنه لو كان كذلك لما كان إثباته من مطالب الكلام، لأن موضوع العلم لا يبين فيه، بل في علم أعلى إلى أن ينتهي إلى ما موضوعه بين الثبوت كالموجود، وذلك لأن حقيقية العلم إثبات الأعراض الذاتية للشيء على ما هو معنى الهلية المركبة، ولا خفاء في أنها بعد الهلية البسيطة لأن ما لا يعلم ثبوته لا يطلب ثبوت شيء له . لكن لا نزاع في أن إثبات الواجب بمعنى إقامة البرهان على وجوده من أعلى مطالب الكلام ، ثم كونه مبدأ الممكنات بالاختيار أو الإيجاب بلا واسطة في الكل أو بواسطة في البعض بحث آخر ـ والقول بأن إثباته إنما هو من مسائل الإلهي دون الكلام ظاهر الفساد، وإلا لكان هو أحد العلوم الإسلامية بل رئيسها، ورأسها ومبنى القواعد الشرعية وأساسها
The subject of a science is not established within it, but rather in what is above it, until it ends with that whose subject is evident in existence, like the existent qua existent. I say: Since among the philosophical investigations there are some that do not harm religious beliefs and are not appropriate for any other Islamic science, the later scholars mixed them with the issues of Kalam in order to spread truths and to benefit from them in escaping from narrow places [?]. Otherwise, there is no dispute that the original Kalam does not exceed the discussions of the Essence, the Attributes, Prophethood, Imamate, and the Return, and what pertains to that from the states of possible beings. Therefore, the early scholars confined themselves to refuting that the subject of Kalam is God alone or with the essence of possible beings from the aspect of dependence, on the grounds that if it were so, then proving Him would be among the demands of Kalam, because the subject of a science is not established within it, but in a higher science until it ends with that whose subject is evident in existence, like the existent. That is because the reality of a science is proving the essential accidents of a thing, which is the meaning of the composite proposition (al-haliyya al-murakkaba), and it is no secret that it is after the simple proposition (al-haliyya al-basita), because what is not known to exist is not asked for the proof of something for it. But there is no dispute that proving the Necessary Being in the sense of establishing the proof of His existence is among the highest demands of Kalam; then that He is the origin of possible beings by choice or by necessitation without mediation in all or with mediation in some is another discussion. The statement that proving Him is from the issues of metaphysics (al-ilāhi) rather than Kalam is clearly corrupt, otherwise it would be one of the Islamic sciences, indeed their chief, their head, the foundation of the religious principles and their basis.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage states a foundational principle of Islamic epistemology: the subject of a science is not proven within that science itself, but in a higher science, until one reaches a science whose subject is self-evident (like existence). The author then explains that the early scholars of kalam limited the subject of kalam to discussions of God, attributes, prophethood, imamate, and eschatology, and they rejected that proving God's existence is part of kalam because that would make God's existence the subject, which would violate the principle. However, the author clarifies that there is no dispute that proving the Necessary Being is among the highest demands of kalam, and he refutes the idea that it belongs to metaphysics instead of kalam, arguing that kalam is the chief of Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
موضوع العلم
الأعراض الذاتية
الهلية المركبة
الهلية البسيطة
الواجب الوجود
الممكنات
الإلهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam does not deal with proving God's existence; that belongs to metaphysics.
Correction: Taftazani argues that proving God's existence is among the highest demands of kalam, and that kalam is the chief of all Islamic sciences, not a subordinate to metaphysics.

Checkpoint
What is the principle stated at the beginning of this passage?
A) The subject of a science is proven within it.
B) The subject of a science is not proven within it, but in a higher science.
C) The subject of kalam is God alone.
D) Proving God's existence is not a part of kalam.

Key Takeaway
The subject of a science is not proven within it; kalam's highest demand is proving the Necessary Being, and it is the chief of all Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now understand that kalam's highest demand is proving the Necessary Being. But have you personally proven His existence to your own satisfaction, or do you rely on the work of scholars? If the proof is not yours, how will it help you when you stand before Him?
?
If the subject of a science is not proven within it but in a higher science, what is the 'higher science' that confirms the existence of God in your own life? Or do you even have that chain?
?
Taftazani argues that kalam is the chief of Islamic sciences. Yet do you give it the same priority in your own learning? If not, what does that say about your devotion to defending the foundations of your faith?

Fourth and Fifth Arguments on the Subject Matter of Kalam
Sanusi presents two arguments that critique the classification of kalam and propose its subject as the existent or known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth and fifth arguments regarding the subject matter of kalam and their implications for the hierarchy of sciences.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is the existent or known, making it a meta-science that examines foundations.

This passage presents two subtle yet powerful arguments that challenge the conventional view of kalam's subject. Sanusi argues that the common principle 'every science has a subject' leads to paradoxes, and he redefines the hierarchy of sciences based on subject universality. Let's read his arguments carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the fourth argument
The fourth argument challenges the universality of 'every science has a subject, principles, and questions'. How does it do that?
The argument shows that the affirmation of the subject's existence and its simple-predication become questions in kalam itself.
3
Identify the fifth argument
The fifth argument concerns the hierarchy of sciences. What does it say about the subject of kalam and its relation to the divine science?
Note that Sanusi distinguishes between rank (higher vs. lower) and nobility (ashraf).
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why Sanusi says that clarifying a legal science's subject does not make kalam a legal science. What is the key point?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما رابعاً : فلأنه لا يبقى قولهم لكل علم موضوع ومبادىء ومسائل على عمومه، لأن معناه التصديق بآنية الموضوع وهلية البسيطة، وقد صار في علم الكلام من جملة المسائل.
وأما خامساً : فلأن تصاعد العلوم، إنما هو بتصاعد الموضوعات فلا معنى لكون علم أعلى من آخر سوى أن موضوعه أعم، فينبغي أن يؤخذ موضوع علم الكلام الموجود أو المعلوم، وإلا فالإلهي أعلى منه رتبة، وإن كان هو أشرف من جهة، وقد عرفت أن ما يبين فيه موضوع علم شرعي أو مبادئه لا يلزم أن يكون علماً شرعياً، بل يكفي كونه تعينياً وعلى وفق الشرع.
فإن قيل : - فقد آل الكلام إلى أن الوجود المخصص لموضوع الصناعة، وإن كان من أغراضه الذاتية لا يبين فيها لكون نظرها مقصوراً على بيان هليته المركبة، بل ولأنه مسلماً في نظرها لكونه بيناً أو مبيناً في صناعة أعلى، وحينئذ يتوجه الإشكال بأن بيانه هناك لا يكون من الهلية المركبة وموضوع هذا العرض الذاتي لا يكون مما هو مسلم الوجود .
قلنا : موضوع الصناعة الأعلى أعم، ووجوده لا يستلزم وجود الأخص فيبين فيها وجود الأخص بأن يبين انقسام الأعم إليه وإلى غيره، وأنه يوجد له هذا القسم، ويكون ذلك عائداً إلى الهلية المركبة للأعم، مثلاً يبين في الإلهي أن بعض الموجود جسم، فيبين وجود الجسم، وفي الطبيعي أن بعض الجسم كرة ، فيبين وجود الكرة .
وعلى هذا القياس ربما يتنبه الفطن من هذا الكلام لنكتة قادحة في بعض ما
And fourthly: because their statement 'every science has a subject, principles, and questions' does not remain universal, because its meaning is affirmation of the existence (أنية) of the subject and its simple-predication (هلية البسيطة), and this has become among the questions in the science of kalam. And fifthly: because the hierarchy of sciences is only by the hierarchy of subjects, so there is no meaning for one science being higher than another except that its subject is more universal. Thus the subject of the science of kalam should be taken as the existent or the known; otherwise, the divine science (الإلهي) is higher in rank, although it [kalam] is nobler in some respect. And you have already known that what clarifies therein the subject of a legal science or its principles does not necessarily become a legal science; rather, it suffices that it be determinative and in accordance with the Law. If it is said: The discussion has led to the point that the specific existence (الوجود المخصص) of the subject of the art, even if it is among its essential purposes, is not clarified in it because its inquiry is confined to explaining its composite-predication (هليته المركبة), and also because it is taken as given in its perspective as being self-evident or clarified in a higher art. Then the problem arises that its clarification there is not of the composite-predication, and the subject of this essential attribute is not something whose existence is conceded. We say: The subject of the higher art is more universal, and its existence does not entail the existence of the more specific. So the existence of the more specific is clarified in it by clarifying the division of the more universal into it and into other [things], and that this division exists for it. That returns to the composite-predication of the more universal. For example, in the divine science it is clarified that some existents are body, so the existence of body is clarified; and in natural science that some bodies are sphere, so the existence of sphere is clarified. And by this analogy, the astute perhaps notices a critical point in some of what...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments (the fourth and fifth) regarding the subject matter of kalam, followed by an objection and its answer. The author first argues (fourthly) that the common statement 'every science has a subject, principles, and questions' is not universal because the affirmation of the subject's existence and its simple-predication (that it is) itself becomes part of kalam's questions. Then (fifthly) he argues that the hierarchy of sciences is based on the universality of their subjects; thus kalam should take 'the existent or the known' as its subject; otherwise the divine science (theology proper) would be higher. Yet kalam is nobler in some respects. He adds that clarifying a legal science's subject in kalam does not make it a legal science; it suffices that it is determinative and lawful. Next comes an objection: the discussion implies that the specific existence of the subject of an art, though an essential goal, is not clarified within that art because its inquiry is limited to composite-predication (the thing having a property), and that existence is taken as given from a higher art. The problem is that clarifying existence in the higher art would not be composite-predication, and the subject's existence would not be conceded. The author answers: the higher art's subject is more universal, and its existence does not entail the existence of the more specific. So the existence of the more specific is clarified by showing the division of the more universal into it and others, and showing that this division actually occurs. This clarification pertains to the composite-predication of the more universal. Examples: the divine science shows that some existents are bodies, thus proving the existence of body; natural science shows that some bodies are spheres, proving the existence of sphere. The author concludes that an astute reader may detect a critical point in some of the preceding discussion.

Key Terms
موضوع
مبادىء
مسائل
هلية البسيطة
تصاعد العلوم
علم أعلى
الإلهي
علم شرعي
تعيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam is a legal science because it discusses the subject of legal sciences.
Correction: Kalam is not a legal science; it only clarifies the subject of legal sciences in a way that is determinative and in accordance with the Law, but it remains a rational science.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue about the statement 'every science has a subject, principles, and questions'?
A) It is completely true.
B) It is not universal because the investigation of the subject's existence and simple predication falls within kalam.
C) It only applies to legal sciences.
D) It applies only to higher sciences.

Key Takeaway
The fourth and fifth arguments show that kalam's subject is the existent or the known, making it a meta-science that even examines the foundations of other sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study kalam, do you treat it as just another subject, or do you realize that it is examining the very foundations of your belief? How does that change your approach to learning it?
?
The passage says kalam is nobler in some respect because it is in accordance with the Law. Does your study of kalam remain anchored in revelation, or do you find yourself leaning purely on rational arguments? What would you need to change to ensure it is 'determinative and in accordance with the Law'?
?
If kalam's subject is the existent or the known, then every science you study is ultimately under its purview. Does that make you more careful about what you learn and how you relate it to your faith? Commit to reflecting on one area of knowledge you haven't considered in light of kalam.

Objection and Answer: How Higher Sciences Prove Existence of Specific Subjects
Sanusi presents an objection and his response regarding how a higher science clarifies the existence of a specific subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a higher science can clarify the existence of a more specific subject for a lower science through division of the universal.

Core Idea
The existence of a specific subject is proven indirectly by dividing the universal subject.

After presenting his arguments, Sanusi anticipates a powerful objection: if a higher science proves the existence of a subject, that proof would not be composite-predication. His answer reveals a key insight about the relationship between sciences and how existence is established through division. Let's examine this intricate exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. What is the core problem? The objector claims that the specific existence of a subject is not clarified in the art itself, and if it were clarified in a higher art, that clarification would not be composite-predication.
Understand the terms: الوجود المخصص (specific existence), هلية مركبة (composite-predication).
2
Read the answer
Sanusi's answer introduces the concept of division of the more universal. How does this solve the problem?
The answer shows that proving the existence of the specific is done by showing that the universal divides into parts, which is a composite-predication of the universal.
3
Study the examples
The examples from divine science (body) and natural science (sphere) illustrate the method. Trace how each example works.
Note that in each case, the higher science shows that some instances of the universal fall under the specific type.
4
Reflect on the concluding hint
Sanusi ends with a hint that an astute reader might detect a critical point in some part of the discussion. What might that be?

According to the answer to the objection, how does a higher science clarify the existence of a more specific subject for a lower science?
A) By directly proving the existence of the more specific subject.
B) By showing the division of the more universal subject into the more specific and other parts.
C) By relying on the lower science to prove its own subject.
D) By assuming the existence of the more specific subject as self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a specific subject is clarified in a higher science through the composite predication of the universal subject dividing into parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new idea in one field, do you ever trace it back to the universal principles it depends on? How often do you settle for a proof within that field without seeking its foundation in a higher science?
?
This lesson shows that even the proof of a subject's existence relies on dividing a broader category. In your own thinking, when have you assumed something existed without checking how it fits into a larger division of reality?
?
Are you willing to humbly admit that your own field of expertise does not provide all the proofs for its own subjects? This means you must trust and learn from higher disciplines. Is your intellectual pride willing to be subordinated to a higher science?
