Footnote (2) specifies that if knowledge does not involve judgment about a relation but merely apprehends a term or the relation without affirming or denying its actuality, it is tasawwur. Footnote (3) gives multiple definitions of tasawwur: psychologists see it as the form of a thing in the mind; logicians as apprehension of essence without judgment; philosophers distinguish a priori (e.g., unity, multiplicity) from a posteriori (e.g., 'human') conceptions. Footnote (4) divides tasdiq into composite (e.g., 'the world is created' involves conceiving subject, predicate, and relation) and simple (mere apprehension of the relation). It ends by noting that tasdiq is a mental act requiring attributing truth (sidq) to the speaker, as opposed to denial (inkār) and falsification (takdhīb). The passage thus establishes the precise meanings of these two categories, which are essential for kalam reasoning.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
مركب
بسيط
إذعان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That assent (tasdiq) is always a simple mental act.
Correction: Tasdiq can be either composite (involving conception of subject, predicate, and relation) or simple (mere apprehension of relation).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a priori conception?
A) Human
B) Unity
C) Animal
D) World

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur is conception without judgment, defined variously; tasdiq is judgment on a relation, either composite or simple, always involving truth-attribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the Oneness of Allah (tawhid), do you merely conceive of it as an abstract idea, or have you genuinely assented to it with your heart and judged it to be true? Which of the two (tasawwur or tasdiq) does your iman currently rely on?
?
The passage says that assent always involves attributing truth to the speaker. When you hear a Qur'anic verse or a hadith, do you instantly assent to it, or do you find yourself hesitating, denying, or falsifying? Where does your soul stand on the truth of Revelation right now?
?
You have just learned that conception can be a priori (innate) or a posteriori (learned). Which of your beliefs about Allah came purely from your innate nature (fitrah), and which came from hearing or learning? Are you using your fitrah correctly, or have you covered it with doubts and arguments?

The Objection of Reflective Knowledge and Its Resolution
Objection and refutation regarding the definition of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between perpetual and temporary inability resolves the apparent contradiction between reflective knowledge and necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
After acquiring theoretical knowledge, you cannot unlearn it, but you could have chosen not to learn it.

When we say necessary knowledge is knowledge we cannot separate from, a challenge arises: what about knowledge gained by reflection? Once you understand something, you cannot unthink it. The scholars answer by clarifying the timing of inability—perpetual versus after acquisition. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection: knowledge by reflection seems to contradict the definition of necessary knowledge because once obtained, one cannot separate from it.
Notice that the objector is using the same criterion (inability to separate) to claim that reflective knowledge is necessary.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
Read the answer carefully. It distinguishes between necessary knowledge (where inability is perpetual) and theoretical knowledge (where inability only occurs after acquisition).
The key phrase is 'نفي القدرة دائماً' vs 'تنتفي القدرة بعد الحصول'.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider an example: knowing that 2+2=4. You cannot unlearn it, but before learning it you could have chosen not to engage in the reasoning. This shows it is theoretical, not necessary.
This solidifies why reflective knowledge does not fall under the definition of necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يرد على طرد العبارتين العلم الحاصل بالنظر، إذ لا قدرة حينئذ على تحصيله ولا على الانفكاك عنه . أجيب بأن المعتبر في الضروري نفي القدرة دائماً، وفي النظري إنما تنتفي القدرة بعد الحصول إذ قبله يقتدر على التحصيل بأن يكتسب وعلى الانفكاك بأن لا يكتسب
If it is said: The objection against the generality of the two phrases is the knowledge obtained by reflection, for there is no power at that time to acquire it nor to separate from it. It is answered: That what is considered in the necessary (knowledge) is the negation of power perpetually, and in the theoretical (knowledge) power is only negated after obtaining, because before it one has the power to acquire by earning and to separate by not earning.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of necessary (ضروري) and theoretical (نظري) knowledge. The objector claims that knowledge obtained by reflection (العلم الحاصل بالنظر) contradicts the definition because once obtained, the knower cannot separate from it, making it seem necessary. The answer clarifies the distinction: in necessary knowledge, the inability to separate is perpetual; in theoretical knowledge, the inability only occurs after acquisition. Before acquisition, one has the power to either acquire or not acquire. Thus the objection is resolved.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if you cannot separate from acquired knowledge, it must be necessary knowledge.
Correction: Necessary knowledge is defined by perpetual inability to separate, whereas theoretical knowledge only has inability after acquisition; before acquisition, you had the power to choose.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what distinguishes necessary knowledge from theoretical knowledge?
A) Necessary knowledge cannot be acquired, while theoretical can.
B) In necessary knowledge, inability to separate is perpetual; in theoretical, it is only after acquisition.
C) Necessary knowledge is known by all, theoretical only by scholars.
D) Theoretical knowledge does not involve power at all.

Key Takeaway
The defining difference between necessary and theoretical knowledge is not the ability to separate after acquisition, but the presence of choice before acquisition.

Acquired, Necessary, and Theoretical Knowledge
Classification of knowledge into acquired (كسبي) and necessary (ضروري), and synonyms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of acquired knowledge and how it relates to necessary and theoretical knowledge in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is not always synonymous with theoretical; its definition depends on the method of acquisition.

This passage from Sanusi's work clarifies the terminology of knowledge classification. It explains that the term 'acquired' (كسبي) can have different shades of meaning depending on the theological framework. By understanding these nuances, you will better grasp the foundation of epistemic categories in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the two perspectives on acquisition.
2
Identify the two perspectives
The passage mentions two views: one where acquisition is only through reflection, and another where it includes voluntary engagement with causes like intellect or senses.
These correspond to different theological schools.
3
Understand the synonym relationship
In the first view, acquired is synonymous with theoretical. In the second, acquired is broader. Note the term 'inferential' (استدلالي) as a specification of theoretical.
4
Apply the concept
Think of examples of acquired knowledge through reflection (like logical deduction) and through direct causes (like sensory perception).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكسبي يقابل الضروري ويرادف النظري فيمن يجعل طريق الاكتساب هو النظر لا غير . وأما فيمن جوز الكسب بمثل التصفية والإلهام من خوارق العادات وقد يقال الكسبي لما يحصل بمباشرة الأسباب اختياراً كصرف العقل أو الحس. والضروري لما يقابله ويخص الكسبي النظري باسم النظري باسم الاستدلالي.
And the acquired (الكسبي) contrasts with the necessary (الضروري) and is synonymous with the theoretical (النظري) in the view of one who makes the way of acquisition reflection only. But as for one who permits acquisition by means such as purification and inspiration from supernatural habits, the acquired is said to be what is obtained by directly engaging the causes voluntarily, such as directing the intellect or sense. And the necessary is what contrasts with it. And the theoretical (النظري) is specified with the name 'inferential' (الاستدلالي).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the terminology. It states that the acquired (الكسبي) is the opposite of the necessary (الضروري) and is synonymous with theoretical (النظري) for those who hold that acquisition is only through reflection. However, for those who allow acquisition through supernatural means like purification and inspiration, the acquired is defined as what is obtained by directly engaging causes voluntarily, such as using the intellect or senses. The necessary is its counterpart. Additionally, the theoretical is specifically called 'inferential' (الاستدلالي). This clarifies the range of meanings of these key terms in kalam.

Key Terms

ضروري
نظري
استدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that acquired knowledge (كسبي) and theoretical knowledge (نظري) are always identical.
Correction: The passage shows that they are synonymous only in one view; in another, acquired can be broader, including knowledge gained through voluntary causes like sensory perception.

Checkpoint

A) It is synonymous with theoretical.
B) It is obtained by directly engaging causes voluntarily.
C) It is the same as necessary.
D) It is only through reflection.

Key Takeaway
The term 'acquired knowledge' has a broader definition that includes voluntary engagement with causes, not merely reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Possibilities and the Problem of Identical Definiens
Sanusi explains the impossibility of identity in definition and introduces three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definiens cannot be identical to the definiendum and the three possibilities (all parts, some parts, external) that arise in definition.

Core Idea
A thing cannot be clearer than itself, so the definiens must be different from the definiendum.

Sanusi begins by setting out a fundamental logical puzzle: how can a definition (al-muʿarrif) make something known if it is the same as the thing being defined? He quickly shows that identity leads to circularity, and then introduces the three possible relationships between definiens and definiendum: all parts, some parts, or external. This passage lays the groundwork for the broader debate on definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the impossibility of identity
Start from 'As for the second' and read until the introduction of the three possibilities. Pay attention to the argument that a thing cannot be clearer than itself.
This is the core of the problem.
2
Identify the three possibilities for definiens
After stating the impossibility, the author lists the three possibilities: all parts, some parts, or external. Note how each possibility leads to further issues.
3
Examine the problem with definition by all parts
The passage then focuses on the first possibility (all parts). Understand why this leads to circularity: if the definiens is all parts, it is identical to the definiendum, so no new knowledge is gained.
The same problem recurs later in the resolution.
4
Review the critique of al-Mawāqif
The author critiques a statement from al-Mawāqif regarding definition by the external. Compare the author's correction with the original.
This highlights the precision required in logical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلومة من جهة بعض عوارضها، وأما الثاني : فلأن الكاسب - أعني المعرف (١) للماهية يمتنع أن يكون نفسها لامتناع كون الشيء أجلى وأسرع معرفة من نفسه بل يكون إما جميع أجزائها وهو نفسها، فيعود المحذور وإما ،بعضها، أو خارجاً عنها. ويندرج فيه المركب من الداخل والخارج ومن أفرده بالذكر أراد بالداخل والخارج المحض من ذلك، ثم البعض إنما يعرف الماهية إذا عرف شيئاً من أجزائها، إذ لو كانت الأجزاء بأسرها معلومة أو بقيت مجهولة لم يكن المعرف معرفاً أي سبباً لمعرفة الماهية وموصلاً إلى تصورها، فالجزء المعرف إن كان نفسه عاد المحذور. وإن كان غيره لزم التعريف بالخارج ضرورة كون كل جزء خارجاً عن الآخر، ولو فرض تداخلها بنقل الكلام إلى تعريفه للجزء منه ومن غيره، فيعود المحذور أو التعريف بالخارج وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الخارج إنما يفيد معرفة الماهية إذا علم اختصاصه بها بمعنى ثبوته لها ففيه عن جميع ما عداها وهذا تصديق يتوقف على تصور الماهية وهو دور، وتصور ما عداها من أمور الغير المتناهية على التفصيل وهو محال، وفي عبارة المواقف هنا تسامح حيث قال - والبعض إن عرف الماهية عرف نفسه وقد أبطل الخارج وسنبطل لأن الذي سيبطل هو التعريف بالخارج لا الخارج، وكأنه على حذف الباء أي عرف بالخارج أو على معنى (۳) أو عرف الأمر الذي شأنه أن يكون خارجاً عن سائر الأجزاء فيكون البعض المعرف خارجاً عنه ويلزم التعريف بالخارج لا الخارج، وإنما ادعى لزوم المحالين على ما هو تقرير المحصل بناء على أن معرف الماهية معرف لكل جزء منها، والظهور المنع عليه، اقتصرنا على أحدهما كما هو تقرير المطالب التالية، لا يخفى أن القدح في بعض مقدمات هذا الاستدلال كاف في دفعه إلا أنهم لما جوزوا التعريف بجميع الأجزاء بالبعض وبالخارج احتجنا إلى التقصي (۳) عن الإشكالات الثلاثة، أما عن الأول فبأن جميع الأجزاء وإن كانت نفس الماهية بالذات، لكن إنما يمتنع التعريف بها لو لم (١) يغايرها بالاعتبار وتحقيقه أن الأجزاء قد تتعلق بها تصورات متعددة بأن تلاحظ واحداً على التفصيل والترتيب، فيكون كاسباً أي حداً وقد يتعلق بها تصور واحد بأن يلاحظ المجموع من حيث المجموع فيكون مكتسباً أي محدوداً، وهذا معنى قولهم في المحدود إجمال وفي الحد تفصيل، ولا امتناع في أن يكون تصور المجموع مترتباً على مجموع التصورات ومسبباً عنها.
The known from the aspect of some of its accidents. As for the second: because the acquirer (al-kāsib) — meaning the definiens (al-muʿarrif) of the essence (al-māhiyyah) — cannot be identical to it, since a thing cannot be clearer and quicker to know than itself. Rather, it is either all of its parts — which is itself, so the problem returns — or some of its parts, or external to it. Included in this is the composite of internal and external; whoever mentions them alone intends the pure internal and external from that. Then the part only defines the essence if it knows something of its parts, for if all the parts were known or remained unknown, the definiens would not be a definiens — that is, a cause for knowing the essence and a means to conceiving it. So if the defining part is itself, the problem returns. If it is other, then definition by the external necessarily follows because each part is external to the other. Even if their interpenetration were assumed, the discussion shifts to defining the part by it and by other, so the problem returns or definition by the external — which is also invalid, because the external only yields knowledge of the essence if its specificity to it is known, meaning its subsistence in it, so it distinguishes it from all else. This is a judgment (taṣdīq) that depends on conceiving the essence — which is a circle — and conceiving all else among infinite things in detail — which is impossible. In the wording of al-Mawāqif there is laxity where it says: 'And the part, if it defines the essence, defines itself, and the external has been nullified and we shall nullify [it]' — because what will be nullified is definition by the external, not the external. It is as if the bā' is omitted, meaning 'defines by the external', or in the sense of 'or defines the thing whose nature is to be external to the remaining parts, so the defining part is external to it, and definition by the external follows, not the external.' They only claimed the necessary occurrence of the two impossibilities according to the formulation of al-Muḥaṣṣal based on the idea that the definiens of the essence defines every part of it, and the denial of that is manifest. We confined ourselves to one of them as per the formulation of the following demands. It is not hidden that attacking some premises of this argument suffices to repel it, but since they allowed definition by all parts, by some parts, and by the external, we need to thoroughly examine the three problems. As for the first: because all parts, even if they are identical to the essence in essence, yet definition by them is only impossible if it does not differ from it by consideration (iʿtibār). Its verification: the parts may have multiple conceptions related to them — by observing one in detail and order, so it becomes an acquirer (kāsib), i.e., a definition (ḥadd); and they may have one conception related to them — by observing the whole as whole (majmūʿ min ḥaythu al-majmūʿ), so it becomes an acquired (muktasab), i.e., a defined (maḥdūd). This is the meaning of their saying: 'In the defined there is totality (ijmāl) and in the definition there is detail (tafṣīl).' There is no impossibility in the conception of the whole being arranged upon the totality of conceptions and caused by them.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the first of three problems (al-ishkālāt al-thalātha) concerning definition (al-taʿrīf). The author explains why the definiens (al-muʿarrif) cannot be identical to the definiendum (al-māhiyyah) and then addresses the specific case where the definiens consists of all the parts of the definiendum. The core insight is that although all the parts are identical to the essence in reality, they can be considered differently: when the parts are observed one by one in detail, they constitute the definition (ḥadd), and when observed as a whole, they constitute the defined (maḥdūd). This difference in consideration (al-iʿtibār) removes the impossibility. The author also critiques a statement in al-Mawāqif, noting a laxity, and clarifies that the real issue is definition by the external (al-taʿrīf bi-l-khārij), not the external itself. The passage ends by affirming that the conception of the whole can be caused by the totality of conceptions of the parts, which is the resolution of the first problem.

Key Terms
الماهية
المعرف
المحدود
الحد
التعريف بالخارج
الكاسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the definiens is all parts of the definiendum, definition is always circular and thus impossible.
Correction: The author shows that this is not necessarily true; the resolution (in the next lesson) involves difference in consideration (iʿtibār).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the definiens not be identical to the definiendum?
A) Because the definiens must be external.
B) Because a thing cannot be clearer than itself.
C) Because the definiendum is unknown.
D) Because the definiens must be partial.

Key Takeaway
The definiens cannot be identical to the definiendum; the three possibilities each face logical issues that require resolution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own study of 'Aqeedah, have you ever accepted a definition that was actually circular, repeating the term you were trying to define? Write down one such example and how Sanusi's argument would expose its flaw.
?
Sanusi shows that defining by external leads to an impossible infinite regress. What external markers (e.g., actions, rituals) do you rely on to define your faith? How can you move from those accidents to a definition based on essence without falling into the errors he describes?
?
Imagine someone asks you, 'What is Islam?' If your answer includes the word 'Islam' itself, you commit the identity error. What would be a valid, non-circular definition that avoids all three problematic possibilities? Be honest with yourself.

Resolving the First Problem: Definition by All Parts via Consideration
Sanusi resolves the first problem of definition by introducing the distinction in consideration (iʿtibār).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how definition by all parts is possible through the distinction between detailed (tafṣīl) and total (ijmāl) conception of the parts.

Core Idea
All parts can define the essence when observed in detail as the definition and as a whole as the defined.

After presenting the three problems of definition, Sanusi now resolves the first one. The key is that although the parts are identical to the essence, they can be considered in two different ways: one by one in detail (tafṣīl) or as a whole (ijmāl). This distinction allows the definiens (detailed parts) to cause knowledge of the definiendum (the whole), avoiding circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the resolution passage
Start from 'لا يخفى أن القدح' and read to the end. Note that the author introduces the three problems again and then focuses on the first.
2
Understand the distinction between kāsib and muktasab
The author explains that when parts are observed in detail, they constitute the definition (kāsib); when observed as a whole, they constitute the defined (muktasab). This is the core of the resolution.
This parallels the terms 'acquirer' and 'acquired'.
3
Grasp the role of iʿtibār (consideration)
The resolution relies on the fact that the same parts can be considered in two ways (detailed vs. total), so the definiens is not identical to the definiendum in consideration.
This is a key logical move.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'ijmāl' and 'tafṣīl'
The author concludes with the famous saying: 'In the defined there is totality, and in the definition there is detail.' This encapsulates the resolution.
This is a standard principle in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلومة من جهة بعض عوارضها، وأما الثاني : فلأن الكاسب - أعني المعرف (١) للماهية يمتنع أن يكون نفسها لامتناع كون الشيء أجلى وأسرع معرفة من نفسه بل يكون إما جميع أجزائها وهو نفسها، فيعود المحذور وإما ،بعضها، أو خارجاً عنها. ويندرج فيه المركب من الداخل والخارج ومن أفرده بالذكر أراد بالداخل والخارج المحض من ذلك، ثم البعض إنما يعرف الماهية إذا عرف شيئاً من أجزائها، إذ لو كانت الأجزاء بأسرها معلومة أو بقيت مجهولة لم يكن المعرف معرفاً أي سبباً لمعرفة الماهية وموصلاً إلى تصورها، فالجزء المعرف إن كان نفسه عاد المحذور. وإن كان غيره لزم التعريف بالخارج ضرورة كون كل جزء خارجاً عن الآخر، ولو فرض تداخلها بنقل الكلام إلى تعريفه للجزء منه ومن غيره، فيعود المحذور أو التعريف بالخارج وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الخارج إنما يفيد معرفة الماهية إذا علم اختصاصه بها بمعنى ثبوته لها ففيه عن جميع ما عداها وهذا تصديق يتوقف على تصور الماهية وهو دور، وتصور ما عداها من أمور الغير المتناهية على التفصيل وهو محال، وفي عبارة المواقف هنا تسامح حيث قال - والبعض إن عرف الماهية عرف نفسه وقد أبطل الخارج وسنبطل لأن الذي سيبطل هو التعريف بالخارج لا الخارج، وكأنه على حذف الباء أي عرف بالخارج أو على معنى (۳) أو عرف الأمر الذي شأنه أن يكون خارجاً عن سائر الأجزاء فيكون البعض المعرف خارجاً عنه ويلزم التعريف بالخارج لا الخارج، وإنما ادعى لزوم المحالين على ما هو تقرير المحصل بناء على أن معرف الماهية معرف لكل جزء منها، والظهور المنع عليه، اقتصرنا على أحدهما كما هو تقرير المطالب التالية، لا يخفى أن القدح في بعض مقدمات هذا الاستدلال كاف في دفعه إلا أنهم لما جوزوا التعريف بجميع الأجزاء بالبعض وبالخارج احتجنا إلى التقصي (۳) عن الإشكالات الثلاثة، أما عن الأول فبأن جميع الأجزاء وإن كانت نفس الماهية بالذات، لكن إنما يمتنع التعريف بها لو لم (١) يغايرها بالاعتبار وتحقيقه أن الأجزاء قد تتعلق بها تصورات متعددة بأن تلاحظ واحداً على التفصيل والترتيب، فيكون كاسباً أي حداً وقد يتعلق بها تصور واحد بأن يلاحظ المجموع من حيث المجموع فيكون مكتسباً أي محدوداً، وهذا معنى قولهم في المحدود إجمال وفي الحد تفصيل، ولا امتناع في أن يكون تصور المجموع مترتباً على مجموع التصورات ومسبباً عنها.
The known from the aspect of some of its accidents. As for the second: because the acquirer (al-kāsib) — meaning the definiens (al-muʿarrif) of the essence (al-māhiyyah) — cannot be identical to it, since a thing cannot be clearer and quicker to know than itself. Rather, it is either all of its parts — which is itself, so the problem returns — or some of its parts, or external to it. Included in this is the composite of internal and external; whoever mentions them alone intends the pure internal and external from that. Then the part only defines the essence if it knows something of its parts, for if all the parts were known or remained unknown, the definiens would not be a definiens — that is, a cause for knowing the essence and a means to conceiving it. So if the defining part is itself, the problem returns. If it is other, then definition by the external necessarily follows because each part is external to the other. Even if their interpenetration were assumed, the discussion shifts to defining the part by it and by other, so the problem returns or definition by the external — which is also invalid, because the external only yields knowledge of the essence if its specificity to it is known, meaning its subsistence in it, so it distinguishes it from all else. This is a judgment (taṣdīq) that depends on conceiving the essence — which is a circle — and conceiving all else among infinite things in detail — which is impossible. In the wording of al-Mawāqif there is laxity where it says: 'And the part, if it defines the essence, defines itself, and the external has been nullified and we shall nullify [it]' — because what will be nullified is definition by the external, not the external. It is as if the bā' is omitted, meaning 'defines by the external', or in the sense of 'or defines the thing whose nature is to be external to the remaining parts, so the defining part is external to it, and definition by the external follows, not the external.' They only claimed the necessary occurrence of the two impossibilities according to the formulation of al-Muḥaṣṣal based on the idea that the definiens of the essence defines every part of it, and the denial of that is manifest. We confined ourselves to one of them as per the formulation of the following demands. It is not hidden that attacking some premises of this argument suffices to repel it, but since they allowed definition by all parts, by some parts, and by the external, we need to thoroughly examine the three problems. As for the first: because all parts, even if they are identical to the essence in essence, yet definition by them is only impossible if it does not differ from it by consideration (iʿtibār). Its verification: the parts may have multiple conceptions related to them — by observing one in detail and order, so it becomes an acquirer (kāsib), i.e., a definition (ḥadd); and they may have one conception related to them — by observing the whole as whole (majmūʿ min ḥaythu al-majmūʿ), so it becomes an acquired (muktasab), i.e., a defined (maḥdūd). This is the meaning of their saying: 'In the defined there is totality (ijmāl) and in the definition there is detail (tafṣīl).' There is no impossibility in the conception of the whole being arranged upon the totality of conceptions and caused by them.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the first of three problems (al-ishkālāt al-thalātha) concerning definition (al-taʿrīf). The author explains why the definiens (al-muʿarrif) cannot be identical to the definiendum (al-māhiyyah) and then addresses the specific case where the definiens consists of all the parts of the definiendum. The core insight is that although all the parts are identical to the essence in reality, they can be considered differently: when the parts are observed one by one in detail, they constitute the definition (ḥadd), and when observed as a whole, they constitute the defined (maḥdūd). This difference in consideration (al-iʿtibār) removes the impossibility. The author also critiques a statement in al-Mawāqif, noting a laxity, and clarifies that the real issue is definition by the external (al-taʿrīf bi-l-khārij), not the external itself. The passage ends by affirming that the conception of the whole can be caused by the totality of conceptions of the parts, which is the resolution of the first problem.

Key Terms
الاعتبار
الكاسب
المكتسب
الإجمال
التفصيل

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes definition by all parts possible?
A) The parts are not really identical to the essence.
B) The parts can be considered in detail (tafṣīl) or as a whole (ijmāl).
C) The definiens is always external.
D) The definiendum is unknown before definition.

Key Takeaway
Definition by all parts is possible through the difference in consideration: the parts in detail are the definition, and the parts as a whole are the defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to define a complex concept in your faith, like 'tawḥīd', do you normally consider its parts (e.g., rubūbiyyah, ulūhiyyah) one by one, or do you treat it as a whole? Which approach leads to deeper understanding?
?
This lesson shows that the same thing can be approached differently without contradiction. Is there a belief you hold that you think is at odds with another, but might be reconciled by considering them from different perspectives?
?
What would change in your learning if you consciously distinguished between analyzing details and grasping the whole? Are you willing to adopt that discipline starting today?

Definition by Parts: Detail and Summary
Explains how parts can be considered as definition or defined by mental consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same parts can function either as a definition (detail) or as the defined (summary) depending on the mode of conceptualization.

Core Idea
Definition is detailed observation of parts; the defined is the whole observed as one.

In logic and kalam, the relationship between a definition and the thing defined is not always straightforward. The same set of parts can be considered in two distinct ways: either as a detailed enumeration that forms a definition, or as a single conceptual whole that is the defined. This passage clarifies that distinction and shows why there is no contradiction between the two.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key distinction
The passage introduces two ways of looking at parts: in detail (one by one in order) which yields a definition, or as a whole totality which yields the defined. Pay attention to the Arabic terms kasib and muktasib.
The definition is called 'acquirer' because it acquires the concept of the defined.
2
Learn the terminology
Note that 'kasib' (acquirer) refers to the definition (hadd), and 'muktasib' (acquired) refers to the defined (mahdud). The saying 'the defined is summary, the definition is detail' encapsulates the relationship.
Summary (ijmal) means viewing the whole at once; detail (tafsil) means enumerating parts.
3
Grasp the logical relationship
The passage emphasizes that there is no impossibility in the conceptualization of the totality being caused by and consequent upon the detailed conceptualizations of the parts. This means the definition (detail) logically precedes the defined (summary).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعريف بها لو لم يغايرها بالاعتبار وتحقيقه أن الأجزاء قد تتعلق بها تصورات متعددة بأن تلاحظ واحداً على التفصيل والترتيب، فيكون كاسباً أي حداً وقد يتعلق بها تصور واحد بأن يلاحظ المجموع من حيث المجموع فيكون مكتسباً أي محدوداً، وهذا معنى قولهم في المحدود إجمال وفي الحد تفصيل، ولا امتناع في أن يكون تصور المجموع مترتباً على مجموع التصورات ومسبباً عنها .
The definition of it [the thing] if it does not differ from it by consideration. Its verification is that parts may have multiple conceptualizations attached to them, such that they are observed one by one in detail and order, so it [the whole] becomes an acquirer, i.e., a definition; and sometimes a single conceptualization attaches to them by observing the totality as a totality, so it becomes an acquired thing, i.e., a defined thing. This is the meaning of their saying: regarding the defined there is summary, and regarding the definition there is detail. And there is no impossibility in the conceptualization of the totality being consequent upon the totality of conceptualizations and caused by them.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines and explains how the same parts can serve either as a definition or as the defined, depending on how they are considered. The author begins by clarifying that if the definition does not differ from the defined in reality, it differs by a mental consideration (i' tibar). Then he explains: parts can have multiple conceptualizations (tasawwurat) attached to them—when they are observed one by one in detail and order, the resulting concept is a definition (hadd), called 'acquirer' (kasib). Alternatively, when the parts are observed as a whole (majmu'), the resulting concept is the defined (mahdud), called 'acquired' (muktasib). This corresponds to the common saying: the defined is summary (ijmal) and the definition is detail (tafsil). The author then reaffirms that there is no contradiction in the conceptualization of the totality being dependent on and caused by the multiple conceptualizations of the parts.

إجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that definition and defined are identical in reality, without any difference.
Correction: They are identical in reality but differ in mental consideration: the definition is the detailed observation of parts, while the defined is the summary observation of the whole.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the definition considered to be 'detail' (tafsil) and the defined 'summary' (ijmal)?
A) Because the definition enumerates parts one by one while the defined considers them as a whole.
B) Because the definition is longer in wording than the defined.
C) Because the defined is known intuitively while the definition requires proof.
D) Because the definition is prior in time.

Key Takeaway
A definition and its defined differ only in mental consideration: the definition is the detailed analysis of parts, while the defined is the holistic summary of those parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you define your beliefs summarily without diving into the detailed parts? Take one core belief you hold and commit to analyzing its components this week, just as this lesson teaches definition through parts.
?
The author insists that the whole cannot be understood without the parts. In your spiritual life, do you treat your daily actions (parts) as disconnected from your overall character (whole)? Write down one action you will adjust today to better align with your desired character summary.
?
This lesson says the conceptualization of the whole is caused by the parts. When you think of Allah's attribute of mercy, do you merely have a summary picture or have you studied its detailed implications? Choose one attribute and resolve to learn its detailed meaning from the Qur'an and Sunnah by the end of the week.

Refuting the Objection from al-Mawaqif on Parts and Whole
Refutes the objection from al-Mawaqif regarding the relation of parts to the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection from al-Mawaqif regarding the relation of parts to the whole in defining a concept, and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between detailed and summary observation.

Core Idea
The same parts, observed differently, yield both the definition and the defined.

In this passage, the author engages with a critique from al-Mawaqif that challenges a key claim in the theory of definition: that assembling parts gives rise to a new conceptualization of the whole. The objection argues that parts themselves constitute the essence, not a separate entity. The author responds by clarifying the role of the intellect and the difference between detailed and holistic observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from al-Mawaqif
Carefully read the opening lines where the objection is stated. Note the claim that when parts are assembled in order, they are the essence itself, not that a separate conceptualization arises.
2
Identify the author's initial response
The author states that the objection's apparent meaning is not a valid critique. Understand why: they never claimed that parts cause a separate entity.
3
Understand the key argument about the intellect
The author argues that the intellect can view a single existent in two ways: as a whole (summary) or piece by piece (detailed). No analogy with external existence is valid because the intellect has freedom.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
See how the author resolves the issue
The resolution: the same things, from the perspective of detailed observation, are a definition; from the perspective of summary observation, they are the defined object. That is the meaning of the earlier statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في المواقف : قدحاً في قولهم لمجموع التصورات يحصل تصور المجموع، والحق أن الأجزاء إذا استحضرت مترتبة حتى حصلت فهي الماهية لا أن ثمة حصول مجموع يوجب حصول تصور شيء آخر هو الماهية، وهذا كالأجزاء الخارجية، إذا حصلت كانت نفس المركب الخارجي لا أمراً يترتب عليه المركب، وظاهره غير قادح لأنهم لا يدعون أن مجموع الأجزاء أمر يوجب حصوله حصول أمر آخر هو تصور المجموع، أعني تصور الماهية، فإن أراد نفي ذلك فباطل لا يشهد له ضرورة ولا برهان بل يكذبه الوجدان ولا عبرة بالقياس على الوجود الخارجي، لأنه لا حجر في تصرفات العقل فله أن يلاحظ الموجود الواحد، تارة جملة، وتارة شيئاً فشيئاً، ولم يرد في حل الإشكال على أن قال الحد مجموع الأمور التي كل واحد منها مقدم، ولا يجدي نفعاً لأن المحدود أيضاً كذلك، فلا بد في بيان المغايرة والسببية من أن يقال تلك الأمور من حيث الملاحظة تفصيلاً حداً وإجمالاً محدوداً وهو معنى كلامهم .
And he said in al-Mawaqif, objecting to their statement that from the totality of conceptualizations the conceptualization of the totality obtains: the truth is that when the parts are present in an ordered sequence until they are obtained, they are the essence, not that there is an obtaining of a totality that necessitates the obtaining of a conceptualization of something else that is the essence. This is like external parts—when they are obtained, they are the external composite itself, not something upon which the composite is consequent. But its apparent meaning is not a valid objection because they do not claim that the totality of parts is something whose obtaining necessitates the obtaining of another thing that is the conceptualization of the totality, i.e., the conceptualization of the essence. If he intends to deny that, it is false, as no intuition or proof witnesses it, rather intuition belies it. And no weight is given to analogy with external existence, because there is no restriction on the operations of the intellect; it may observe a single existent sometimes as a whole and sometimes piece by piece. And he did not succeed in solving the problem in that he said: the definition is the totality of things each of which is a premise; this is of no benefit because the defined is also such. Therefore, in explaining the difference and causality, one must say: those things, from the perspective of detailed observation, are a definition, and from the perspective of summary observation, are a defined object, and that is the meaning of their statement.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents a critique from al-Mawaqif and its refutation. The critique claims that when parts are assembled, they themselves constitute the essence, not a separate mental concept. The author responds that this critique does not harm the original position, because they never claimed that the parts cause a separate entity; rather, the conceptualization of the whole is the same as the conceptualization of the parts when observed as a whole. He argues that the intellect can view one existent in two ways—as a whole or piecemeal—and there is no contradiction. The critique's attempt to define the definition as a set of premises is also rejected because the defined can be described similarly. The resolution is to say that the same things are a definition when observed in detail and a defined when observed in summary.

Key Terms
مواقف
ماهية
مجموع
تصور
مقدم
سببية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that assembling parts creates a new mental entity separate from the parts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the parts themselves, when considered as a whole, constitute the essence—no separate entity is caused. The intellect simply views the same parts in different modes.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection from al-Mawaqif according to this passage?
A) That the parts are not known individually.
B) That the assembly of parts gives rise to a separate essence.
C) That the parts when assembled are the essence itself, not a cause for a separate mental concept.
D) That definition cannot be acquired.

Key Takeaway
The refutation hinges on the intellect's ability to consider the same set of parts in two different modes: detailed (as definition) and summary (as defined).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you tend to grasp theological concepts in a summary fashion without examining their parts in detail? Which key belief of yours might be superficial because you have not broken it down? What will you do this week to study one aspect of your faith in greater detail?
?
The intellect can view one thing in two ways. When you encounter a difficult question about Allah's attributes or predestination, do you lock into one perspective and resist shifting? How might switching between 'detailed' and 'summary' observation deepen your understanding and remove confusion? Commit to a specific question you will approach with both modes.
?
If the same parts can be both definition and defined depending on how you look, what does that say about your own intellectual habits? Are you quick to define something—a person, an issue—based on a single perspective? Repent of having a rigid mind and ask Allah to grant you the freedom of observation described here.

First Objection: Knowledge of Parts Suffices?
Objection and answer on whether parts suffice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the definition of kalam and its resolution: that scattered knowledge of parts requires ordered reflection (nazar) to constitute acquisition.

Core Idea
Knowing parts alone doesn't guarantee knowing the whole; they must be gathered in order.

This lesson addresses a crucial objection to the definition of kalam as the science that seeks to acquire knowledge of the whole from its parts. The objection claims that if the parts are already known, the whole is known without effort, rendering definition unnecessary. Sanusi provides a nuanced answer: parts may be scattered among other known things, and reflection is needed to assemble them into an ordered sequence that yields conceptualization of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the dilemma posed: if parts are known, whole is known; if not, definition fails.
Notice the two horns of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The objection presents two cases: parts already obtained (so no acquisition) or parts not obtained (so they are sought, not the whole). Understand why both challenge the need for definition.
3
Analyze the answer
Sanusi responds that parts may be known but scattered. Reflect on what 'scattered' means: the parts exist in the mind individually but are not connected.
The key is that acquisition is the act of ordering.
4
Understand 'al-juz' al-sadri'
The outcome of acquisition is obtaining the 'first part' of the definition. This is the part that, when combined with others through the definition, yields conceptualization of the whole essence.
This concept ties the objection to the standard view of definition.
5
Generalize to assents
The objection also applies to assents (tasdiqat). Reflect on how the same logic works for acquiring beliefs from premises.
Sanusi says 'and analogize accordingly', inviting broader application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا كان مجموع التصورات مفضياً إلى تصور المجموع، فإن كانت حاصلة كان هو أيضاً حاصلاً من غير أثر للنظر والاكتساب، وإن لم تكن حاصلة لم يصلح معرفاً بل تكون مطلوبة وينقل الكلام إلى ما يحصلها، وكذا الكلام في التعريف ببعض الأجزاء أو بالخارج، بل في اكتساب التصديقات . قلنا : يجوز أن تكون الأجزاء معلومة منتشرة في سائر المعلومات فيفتقر إلى النظر لاستحضار مجموعة مترتبة بحيث تفضي إلى تصور الماهية وهذا معنى الاكتساب وحاصله عائد إلى تحصيل الجزء الصدري بالحد، وعلى هذا فقس .
For it is said: If the totality of conceptualizations leads to the conceptualization of the totality, then if they [the parts] are already obtained, it [the totality's conceptualization] would also be obtained without any effect of reflection and acquisition; and if they are not obtained, it [the totality] would not be a valid definiens, but rather would be sought, and the discussion would shift to what obtains them. The same applies to definition by some parts or by what is external, and even to the acquisition of assents. We say: It is possible that the parts are known but scattered among other known things, so reflection is needed to bring them together in an ordered sequence that leads to the conceptualization of the essence. This is the meaning of acquisition, and its outcome reduces to obtaining the first part [of the definition] by the definition. And analogize accordingly.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection and its answer. The objection argues: if the parts conceptualizations lead to the whole conceptualization, then either the parts are already known, so the whole is known without effort—eliminating the need for a definition—or the parts are unknown, so the definition would not help. The answer clarifies that parts may be known individually but scattered among many known things; acquisition consists in gathering them into an ordered sequence that yields the conceptualization of the essence. The outcome is obtaining the first part (al-juz' al-sadri) via the definition.

Key Terms
اكتساب
نظر
تصور
جزء صدري
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If all parts of a concept are individually known, the whole concept is automatically known without any additional effort.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that parts may be known but scattered among other known items; reflection (nazar) is needed to order them into a sequence that yields the conceptualization of the whole.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, why is reflection needed even if the parts are known?
A) Because the parts are unknown until reflection.
B) Because the parts are scattered among other known things and need to be gathered in order.
C) Because reflection creates new parts.
D) Because the definition is external to the parts.

Key Takeaway
The first objection is resolved by recognizing that known parts may be scattered, so acquisition consists in ordered gathering, not merely knowing the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may know many isolated facts about your faith, but do you have an ordered conceptualization of your belief in Allah that truly shapes your life? In what area of your dīn are your pieces of knowledge still scattered, and when will you deliberately sit to order them through reflection?
?
The 'first part' of a definition is foundational. What is the first part of your understanding of Tawḥīd? Have you clearly defined your core belief, or is it vague? How does that affect your worship and reliance on Allah?
?
Consider a major concept in your faith, such as the Hereafter. You know many details about it, but are those details ordered in your mind to produce a coherent, motivating conceptualization? If not, what scattered information needs to be gathered, and what action will you take today to start that ordering?

Definiens Need Not Specify Parts
Refutes the claim that definition must specify parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a definition can make an essence known without specifying its parts, and how the author defends this using the example of a social form.

Core Idea
A definition can bring the whole to mind without detailing every part.

In this passage, the author answers a critical objection: if a definition must make known some part of the defined, then how can it ever succeed when parts are unknown? The response is subtle: the definiens provides an ordered, distinct awareness of the whole, even if the parts are already known in isolation. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the author's compact argumentative style.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core claim and denial
The objection claims that the definiens must make known some part of the essence. The author denies this. Find the sentence where he says 'we do not concede' and the reason he gives.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between knowledge of the essence and knowledge of its parts. He says even if the essence is ontologically the parts, knowing it is not the same as knowing the parts individually. Why?
Focus on the phrase 'knowledge of it is not knowledge of the parts'.
4
Follow the refutation of three sub-objections
The author then answers three specific objections that arise from the initial one. Trace each one: (1) parts are the essence, (2) definiendum as cause, (3) cause of whole vs parts. Note the social form analogy at the end.
5
Reflect on the social form analogy
The author uses the example of a social form that causes the composite but not its individual parts. How does this illustrate the point? Write a short explanation.
This is a powerful analogy for understanding causal relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الثاني : فبأنا لا نسلم أن معرف الماهية يجب أن يعرف شيئاً من أجزائها، لجواز أن تكون الأجزاء معلومة، ونفتقر إلى حضورها مجموعة مترتبة ممتازة عما عداها ويكون ذلك بالمعرف وحاصله أن الماهية وإن كانت نفس الأجزاء بحسب الذات لكن لا يلزم أن يكون العلم بها هو العلم بالأجزاء، بمعنى التصورات المتعلقة بها، بل لا بد من ملاحظتها مجتمعة متميزة عن الأغيار، ويجوز أن تبقى الأجزاء مجهولة ويفيد المعرف بالذات، ويعود التغاير إلى الإجمال والتفصيل، كما في تعريف الماهية بأجزائها أو غيره، ويصح التعريف بالخارج على ما سيجيء وبما ذكرنا يندفع ما يقال إن أجزاء جميع الماهية نفسها، فكيف لا يكون العلم بها علماً بها؟ وأن معرف الشيء سبب معرفته، أي حصوله في الذهن، فكيف لا يحصل شيئاً من أجزائه؟ وأن علة حصول الشيء لو لم تكن علة لشيء من أجزائه لجاز حصول كل جزء بدونه، فجاز حصول الكل بدونه فلم يكن علة ولنعتبر بالهيئة الاجتماعية فإنها علة الحصول المركب وليست علة لحصول شيء من أجزائه .
As for the second [objection]: we do not concede that the definiens of the essence must make known something of its parts, because it is possible that the parts are known, and we need to have them present as an ordered set distinguished from other things, and that is achieved by the definiens. Its conclusion is that even if the essence is ontologically the same as the parts, it does not follow that knowledge of it is knowledge of the parts in the sense of the conceptualizations attached to them; rather, one must observe them as a collective set distinguished from others. And it is possible that the parts remain unknown and the definiens benefits the essence itself, and the difference returns to summary and detail, as in the definition of the essence by its parts or by something else. And definition by external things is valid, as will come. And by what we have mentioned, the following objections are refuted: (1) that the parts of every essence are the essence itself, so how is knowledge of it not knowledge of it? (2) that the definiendum of a thing is the cause of its being known, i.e., its obtaining in the mind, so how does it not obtain something of its parts? (3) that if the cause of obtaining a thing were not a cause of some of its parts, it would be possible for each part to obtain without it, so the whole could obtain without it, so it would not be a cause; consider the social form (al-hay'ah al-ijtima'iyyah), for it is a cause of obtaining the composite but not a cause of obtaining any of its parts.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection: that a definition must make known some part of the defined. The author denies this, arguing that parts may already be known, but we need them to be present in an ordered, distinct set. Knowledge of the essence is not identical to knowledge of its individual parts; rather, it is knowledge of them as a unified whole distinguished from others. The definiens can be the cause of knowing the essence without specifying any part, and the only difference is between summary and detail. Three specific objections are then refuted: (1) essence is its parts, so knowing parts is knowing essence—answer: knowledge of parts individually is different. (2) The definiendum causes knowledge, so it should cause knowledge of some part—answer: that is not necessary, as the definiendum can cause knowledge of the whole. (3) If the cause of the whole does not cause any part, each part could be had without it—answer: analogy with social form (e.g., a composition) where the form causes the composite but not its parts individually.

Key Terms
معرف
نفس الأجزاء
مجتمعة
متميزة
هيئة اجتماعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition must make known at least one specific part or component of the defined thing.
Correction: The definiens can make the whole known without specifying any part, as long as it presents the parts as an ordered, distinguished set.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that if the cause of the whole is not a cause of its parts, the parts could exist without it?
A) By denying that parts can exist without the whole.
B) By giving the example of a social form that causes the composite but not its individual parts.
C) By stating that the whole is prior to its parts.
D) By asserting that definition is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A definition can make an essence known by presenting its parts as an ordered whole, without needing to specify any particular part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you claim to 'know' deeply, like a skill or a person. Do you know it as a collection of facts (parts) or as an integrated whole? Which kind of knowledge truly shapes your actions, and why? How can you train yourself to seek the whole rather than just the parts?
?
The author distinguishes between knowing parts individually and knowing them as an ordered whole. In your own life, when you try to understand a concept, do you tend to break it into parts or grasp it as a whole? Which approach has been more effective for you? What would change if you deliberately practiced holistic understanding?
?
The analogy of social form shows that the cause of the whole is not the cause of its parts. Think of your own character—what is the 'social form' that integrates your traits? Are you aware of that form, or do you only focus on individual habits? How can you intentionally cultivate the whole that gives your life coherence and purpose?

Introduction to the Six Investigations of al-Nazar
The author introduces the chapter on speculative reasoning and lists its six subtopics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the chapter on speculative reasoning (al-nazar) and the six key investigations it covers.

Core Idea
The six investigations of al-nazar form a roadmap for Islamic speculative theology.

This lesson introduces the third chapter of the text, which is devoted to al-nazar (speculative reasoning). The author provides a concise list of six investigations that will be explored in the following sections. Understanding this overview helps you see the structure before delving into the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author lists six numbered investigations. These are the main topics of the chapter.
2
Identify the six investigations
List the six investigations in order: (1) conception and definition, (2) benefit and division into sound/unsound, (3) condition, (4) arriving at the Necessary, (5) first obligation or not, (6) division into concept and assent.
Memorizing these will help you navigate the chapter.
3
Reflect on the logical progression
Consider why the chapter starts with definition, then moves to benefit, condition, and then the ultimate goal (arriving at the Necessary) and the debate about obligation, and finally the division. This shows a systematic approach.
The order is not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في النظر وفيه مباحث ۱ - في تصوره وتعريفه ٢ - في افادته في تقسيمه إلى صحيح وفاسد ٣ - في شرطه ٤ - في التوصل به إلى الواجب إجماعاً ٥ - في أنه أول واجب أم لا ٦ - في انقسامه إلى التصور والتصديق.
The third chapter on speculative reasoning (al-nazar), and it contains investigations: 1. On its conception and definition; 2. On its benefit in its division into sound and unsound; 3. On its condition; 4. On arriving thereby to the Necessary [Existent] by consensus; 5. On whether it is the first obligation or not; 6. On its division into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and a table of contents. It introduces the third chapter of the book, which is on 'al-nazar' (speculative reasoning). It lists six investigations: (1) its conception and definition; (2) its benefit and its division into sound (sahih) and unsound (fasid); (3) its condition (shart); (4) how to arrive through it to the Necessary Existent (al-wajib) by consensus; (5) whether it is the first obligation (awwal wajib) or not; (6) its division into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). These are the topics that will be covered in the chapter.

Misconception: The six investigations listed are the only topics covered in the chapter.
Correction: They are the main headings, but each investigation includes detailed sub-discussions.

B) Investigation 4 (arriving at the Necessary)
C) Investigation 5 (first obligation or not)

Key Takeaway
The chapter on speculative reasoning is structured around six key investigations, from definition and benefit to obligation and division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is one assumption you hold about God that you have never critically examined, and will you examine it today?
?
This lesson states that nazar leads to the Necessary Existent 'by consensus'. Does your own certainty about God's existence rest on a disciplined intellectual path, or on inherited belief? What would it cost you to actually walk through these six investigations for yourself?
?
Investigation 5 asks whether nazar is 'the first obligation'. If it is, then seeking knowledge of God is your most urgent task. Does your life—your time, your thoughts, your choices—reflect that urgency? What would you need to change to make the pursuit of God your actual first concern?

The Reality of Nazar: Two Movements
Defines nazar as the sum of two mental movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nazar (rational consideration) consists of two movements: from the desired objective to its principles, and back from principles to the objective.

Core Idea
Nazar is the sum of two movements, not a single act.

The author begins by describing the mental process of pursuing a desired objective. He identifies two key movements of the soul that together constitute nazar. Understanding this dual nature is essential before exploring further definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice the description of the soul's movement and the mention of 'two movements'.
The passage uses the phrase 'فههنا حركتان'.
2
Identify the two movements
Find how the author describes the first movement (from the desired objective seeking principles) and the second movement (returning with arrangement to attain the objective).
3
Understand the reality of nazar
Note the statement 'وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين' – the reality of nazar is the sum of the two movements. This is the core definition.
Other components like observations and removal of obstacles are part of the process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
في تصوره وتعريفه
إذا حاولنا تحصيل مطلوب فالنفس تتحرك منه في معقولاتها طلباً لمبادئه وتعيين ثم ترجع ههنا ترتيباً وتأدياً إلى المطلوب فههنا حركتان وملاحظات وترتيب وإزالة للموانع، وتوجه إلى المطلوب وغاية للحركة، وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين، لكن قد يكتفى ببعض الأجزاء أو اللوازم، فتفسر بالحركة الأولى أو الثانية أو ترتيب المعلومات للتأدي إلى مجهول، أو ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول، أو تجريد الذهن عن الغفلات أو تحديق العقل نحو المعقولات أو الفكر الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن ويراد بالفكر حركة النفس في المعاني، فيخرج ما يكون لطلب علم أو ظن كأكثر حديث النفس، ويدخل ما يكون لطلب تصور أو تصديق جازم أو راجح من غير ملاحظة المطابقة وعدمها
أقول : أورد فيه ستة مباحث: أولها في بيان حقيقته، ولا خفاء في أن كل مطلوب لا يحصل من أي مبدأ يتفق بل لا بد من مبادىء مناسبة له، والمبادىء لا توصل إليه كيف اتفقت بل لا بد من هيئة مخصوصة فإذا حاولنا تحصيل
The First Investigation: On its conceptualization and definition. When we attempt to obtain a desired objective, the soul moves from it among its intelligibles seeking its principles and determination, then returns here arranging and attaining to the desired objective. So here there are two movements, observations, arrangement, removal of obstacles, direction towards the desired objective, and a goal of the movement. The reality of nazar (consideration) is the sum of the two movements, but one may suffice with some parts or necessary consequences. So it is interpreted as the first movement or the second, or the arrangement of information to attain to an unknown, or the observation of the intelligible to obtain the unknown, or abstraction of the mind from heedlessness, or focusing the intellect towards the intelligibles, or the thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought. And by thought (fikr) is meant the movement of the soul among meanings. Thus what is for seeking knowledge or opinion, like most inner speech, is excluded; and what is for seeking a conceptualization (tasawwur) or a certain or preponderant assent (tasdiq) without considering correspondence or non-correspondence is included. I say: I have included in it six investigations: the first is on explaining its reality. And it is not hidden that every desired objective is not obtained from any principle that happens, but rather there must be suitable principles for it, and principles do not lead to it in any way, but rather there must be a specific arrangement. So when we attempt to obtain...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage defines nazar (rational consideration) and its components. The author begins by describing the process of seeking a desired conclusion: the soul moves from the desired objective seeking its principles, then returns arranging them to reach the objective. There are two movements. The reality of nazar is the sum of these two movements, but it can be defined more narrowly by one movement or other aspects. Various definitions are given. Then the author clarifies that thought (fikr) is distinct from idle speech. The passage concludes with the author's plan to discuss six investigations, starting with the reality of nazar, emphasizing that every objective requires appropriate principles and arrangement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar is only the first movement or only the second movement.
Correction: The passage states that nazar is the sum of both movements, though one may suffice with some parts or necessary consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of nazar?
A) The first movement only
B) The second movement only
C) The sum of the two movements
D) The removal of obstacles

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the combined process of moving from an objective to its principles and back, not just one step.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand a theological truth, do you consciously trace the two movements of nazar in your mind? Which movement do you neglect more often—the initial search for principles or the disciplined return to arrangement? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen the weaker movement?
?
The passage mentions 'removing obstacles' (إزالة الموانع). What internal obstacles—such as pride, laziness, or attachment to an opinion—prevent you from properly executing nazar when studying matters of creed? How will you actively remove at least one of them starting tomorrow?
?
The soul moves from the matlub to principles and back. Are you willing to humbly return to principles when your conclusion is uncertain, or do you cling to your initial opinion out of stubbornness? Recall a recent disagreement you had about religion—did you truly go back to the foundational principles, or did you defend your position without revisiting them? Commit to a specific change in how you handle such situations.

Defining Nazar: Broader Interpretations and the Role of Fikr
Lists alternative definitions of nazar and distinguishes it from fikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various ways nazar is defined and how it is distinguished from mere thought (fikr) and idle speech.

Core Idea
Nazar can be defined by one movement or by its purpose, but it is always a purposeful intellectual act.

After establishing the two-movement structure, the author presents alternative definitions that focus on one part or a necessary aspect. He then clarifies that nazar is a specific type of thought (fikr) aimed at knowledge or opinion, excluding random inner speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative definitions
The passage lists several interpretations of nazar: first movement, second movement, arrangement of information, observation of the intelligible, etc. Read each one carefully.
These definitions are not contradictory but highlight different aspects.
2
Understand the distinction from fikr
Note how the author defines fikr as 'the movement of the soul among meanings' and then excludes what is for seeking knowledge or opinion like most inner speech. Nazar is a subset of fikr.
3
Recognize the broader framework
The author mentions six investigations (mabahith) and begins the first one on the reality of nazar. This shows the systematic structure of the work.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point about needing appropriate principles and arrangement is clear.

Definition of Speculative Reasoning
The author provides a foundational definition of nazar and outlines its dual-movement nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic definition of speculative reasoning (al-nazar) and why it is understood as the sum of two movements.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning is not just thinking; it is a deliberate two-fold movement of the soul.

In this passage, the author begins by defining what speculative reasoning (al-nazar) is. He explains that when we seek to know something, the soul moves in a specific way: first towards the principles, then returns to arrange them. This two-movement structure is essential. The lesson introduces this basic concept and presents various ways the definition is expressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين' – this is the core definition.
2
Identify the two movements
The passage mentions two movements at the outset: the soul moves from the desired object to seek principles, then returns to arrange them. Notice how these are described as 'حركتان'.
3
Understand the reality of nazar
The author states that the reality (haqiqa) of speculative reasoning is the sum of the two movements. Ask yourself why both are necessary.
4
Examine the alternative definitions
The author lists several ways nazar is interpreted, focusing on parts or concomitants. See how each definition highlights a different aspect.
Examples: 'the first movement', 'the second movement', 'arrangement of known things', etc.
5
Reflect on the completeness
Consider why a definition that includes both movements is more complete than one that focuses on a part. What might be lost if only one movement is considered?

المبحث الأول في تصوره وتعريفه إذا حاولنا تحصيل مطلوب فالنفس تتحرك منه في معقولاتها طلباً لمبادئه وتعيين ثم ترجع ههنا ترتيباً وتأدياً إلى المطلوب فههنا حركتان وملاحظات وترتيب وإزالة للموانع، وتوجه إلى المطلوب وغاية للحركة، وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين، لكن قد يكتفى ببعض الأجزاء أو اللوازم، فتفسر بالحركة الأولى أو الثانية أو ترتيب المعلومات للتأدي إلى مجهول، أو ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول، أو تجريد الذهن عن الغفلات أو تحديق العقل نحو المعقولات أو الفكر الذي يطلب به علم أو ظن ويراد بالفكر حركة النفس في المعاني، فيخرج ما يكون لطلب علم أو ظن كأكثر حديث النفس، ويدخل ما يكون لطلب تصور أو تصديق جازم أو راجح من غير ملاحظة المطابقة وعدمها) أقول : أورد فيه ستة مباحث: أولها في بيان حقيقته، ولا خفاء في أن كل مطلوب لا يحصل من أي مبدأ يتفق بل لا بد من مبادىء مناسبة له، والمبادىء لا توصل إليه كيف اتفقت بل لا بد من هيئة مخصوصة فإذا حاولنا تحصيل مطلوب تصوري أو تصديقي ولا محالة يكون مشعوراً به من وجه تحرکت النفس منه في الصور المخزونة عندها، منتقلاً من صورة إلى صورة إلى أن يظفر بمبادئه من الذاتيات والعرضيات والحدود الوسطى، فيستحضرها متعينة متميزة ، ثم يتحرك فيها لترتيبها ترتيباً خاصاً يؤدي إلى تصور المطلوب بحقيقته، أو بوجه يمتاز به عما عداه، أو إلى التصديق به يقيناً أو غير يقين، فههنا حركتان يحصل بأولاهما المادة ، وبالثانية : الصورة والمبادىء من حيث الوصول إليها منتهى الحركة الأولى، ومن حيث الرجوع عنها مبدأ الحركة الثانية، ومن حيث التصرف فيها لترتب الترتيب المخصوص مادة الثانية، وحقيقة النظر مجموع الحركتين وهما من جنس الحركة في الكيف بتوارد الصور والكيفيات على النفس، ولا محالة يكون هناك توجه نحو المطلوب، وإزالة لما [؟] والصور المضادة والمنافية وملاحظة للمعقولات ليؤخذ البعض ويحذف البعض، وترتيب للمأخوذ وغاية يقصد حصولها، وكثيراً ما يقتصر في تفسير النظر على بعض أجزائه، أو لوازمه اكتفاء بما يفيد امتيازه أو اصطلاحاً على ذلك، فيقال : هو
The first topic: on its conceptualization and definition. When we try to obtain a desired thing, the soul moves from it among its intelligibles seeking its principles and determination, then returns here in arrangement and delivery to the desired thing. So here there are two movements, observations, arrangement, removal of obstacles, direction toward the desired thing, and a goal for the movement. The reality of speculative reasoning (al-nazar) is the sum of the two movements, but sometimes one is satisfied with some parts or concomitants. Thus it is interpreted as the first movement or the second, or as the arrangement of known things to arrive at an unknown, or as observation of the intelligible to obtain the unknown, or as emptying the mind of heedlessness, or as focusing the intellect toward the intelligibles, or as the thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought – and by 'thought' is meant the movement of the soul among meanings. So what is for seeking knowledge or opinion, like most inner speech, is excluded, and what is for seeking a concept or a definitive or preponderant assent without regard to correspondence or non-correspondence is included. I say: He presented in it six investigations; the first of them is on stating its reality. There is no hiddenness in that every desired thing is not obtained from any principle that happens to occur; rather, principles appropriate to it are necessary, and principles do not lead to it however they happen to be; rather, a specific configuration is necessary. So when we try to obtain a conceptual or assented desired thing, necessarily it is perceived in some respect; the soul moves from it among the forms stored with it, passing from one form to another until it obtains its principles from the essences, accidents, and middle terms. Then it brings them to mind as determinate and distinct. Then it moves within them to arrange them in a specific arrangement that leads to the conceptualization of the desired thing in its reality, or in a respect distinguishing it from others, or to assent to it with certainty or without certainty. So here there are two movements: by the first, the material (al-mādda) is obtained, and by the second, the form (al-ṣūra). And the principles, from the perspective of reaching them, are the end of the first movement, and from the perspective of returning from them, are the beginning of the second movement, and from the perspective of disposing within them to arrange the specific arrangement, are the material of the second movement. The reality of speculative reasoning is the sum of the two movements, and they are of the genus of movement in quality by the successive occurrence of forms and qualities upon the soul. And necessarily there is direction toward the desired thing, removal of what [?] and opposing and contradictory forms, observation of intelligibles so that some are taken and some are discarded, and arrangement of what is taken, and a goal intended to be attained. And often the interpretation of speculative reasoning is restricted to some of its parts or its concomitants, relying on what distinguishes it or on convention regarding that, and so it is said: it is...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage defines speculative reasoning (al-nazar) by explaining its nature and two essential movements. The author begins by stating that when one seeks to obtain a desired object of knowledge (conceptual or assented), the soul moves from that object among its stored intelligibles. It first seeks the principles (mabādiʾ) relevant to the desired object and then returns to arrange them in order to arrive at the desired result. This process involves two movements, along with observations, arrangement, removal of obstacles, direction toward the goal, and the goal itself. The reality of nazar is the sum of these two movements, though sometimes it is defined by only one part or a necessary concomitant, leading to various definitions: as the first movement alone, the second alone, the arrangement of knowns to reach an unknown, observation of the intelligible to obtain an unknown, emptying the mind of heedlessness, focusing the intellect, or thought that seeks knowledge or opinion. Thought is defined as the movement of the soul among meanings, excluding random inner speech and including the pursuit of a concept or a definitive or preponderant judgment without yet regarding correspondence to reality. Then the commentator (the author) says: 'I say: He presented in it six investigations; the first is on stating its reality.' The author then explains that any desired thing requires appropriate principles arranged in a specific way. The process begins with the soul moving from a partially perceived desired thing through stored forms until it finds the necessary principles—essences, accidents, and middle terms. It then arranges them in a specific order to produce either a concept (tasawwur) of the thing's essence or a distinguishing mark, or an assent (tasdiq) that is certain or less than certain. This yields two movements: the first obtains the material (al-mādda), i.e., the principles; the second imposes the form (al-ṣūra) by arranging them. The principles, when reached, are the end of the first movement; when used as a starting point for arrangement, they are the beginning of the second; and when disposed for the specific order, they are the material of the second. The reality of nazar is the combination of both movements, which are a type of qualitative change through the succession of forms and qualities in the soul. Additionally, there must be direction, removal of obstacles (including opposing and contradictory forms), selection of some intelligibles and discarding others, arrangement of what is taken, and a final goal. Often definitions restrict nazar to some parts or concomitants for the sake of distinguishing it or by convention, resulting in various sayings such as 'it is...' (the text breaks off). The passage thus provides a comprehensive analysis of what constitutes valid speculative reasoning in Islamic theology and logic.

Key Terms
النظر
الحركتان
المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality (haqiqa) of speculative reasoning (al-nazar)?
A) The first movement alone
B) The second movement alone
C) The sum of the two movements
D) The removal of obstacles

Key Takeaway
Speculative reasoning (al-nazar) is defined as the composite of two movements: from the desired object to its principles and back.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last tried to understand a difficult religious concept, did you deliberately move from the concept to find its principles (e.g., verses, proofs) and then return to the concept with those principles? Or did you rely on random thoughts and feelings?
?
This lesson teaches that true nazar includes removing obstacles (izalat al-mawani'). What obstacles—such as laziness, arrogance, or distractions—prevent you from disciplining your mind in the pursuit of knowledge? Are you willing to remove them today?
?
The passage distinguishes nazar from mere inner speech (hadith al-nafs). How much of your mental energy is spent on aimless thinking versus intentional reasoning? Will you commit to making at least one focused 'nazar' session this week?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
The Two Movements of Speculative Reasoning
The author provides a detailed explanation of the two movements of nazar, emphasizing material and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed process of speculative reasoning, including the roles of material and form, and the necessity of appropriate principles and arrangement.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning obtains its material in the first movement and imposes form in the second.

The commentary section (أقول) delves deeper into the two movements. It explains that any desired object requires appropriate principles arranged in a specific way. The soul first seeks these principles (material) and then arranges them (form) to achieve conceptualization or assent. This lesson unpacks that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
This passage is the author's commentary (أقول). Read it carefully, noticing how it builds on the earlier definition.
2
Understand the necessity of appropriate principles and arrangement
The passage stresses that not just any principles will do; they must be suitable and arranged in a specific way. This is the foundation of the two movements.
3
Identify the two movements and their products
The first movement obtains the material (al-madda), i.e., the principles. The second movement obtains the form (al-sura) by arranging them. See how the author describes this: 'يحصل بأولاهما المادة، وبالثانية: الصورة'.
4
Note the detailed components
The author lists additional elements: direction, removal of obstacles, observation, selection, arrangement, and a goal. Recognize that all these are part of the process.
5
Consider why the definition is often truncated
The passage ends by noting that interpretations of nazar often restrict it to parts or concomitants. Reflect on why a comprehensive definition might be simplified in practice.
The text ends abruptly with 'فيقال : هو' indicating that further explanations follow elsewhere.

Commentary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speculative reasoning is a single, simple mental act.

Checkpoint
In the definition of speculative reasoning (al-nazar), what is obtained by the first movement?
A) The form (al-ṣūra)
B) The material (al-mādda)
C) The goal (al-ghāya)
D) The arrangement (al-tartīb)

Key Takeaway
The two movements of nazar correspond to acquiring the material (principles) and organizing them into a form that yields knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you set out to understand a complex theological issue, do you intentionally gather the necessary principles (scriptural texts, logical premises, etc.) before arranging your thoughts, or do you tend to leap to conclusions? What would change if you consciously practiced the two movements of nazar?
?
The passage says nazar involves 'removal of what opposes' and 'observation of intelligibles to take some and discard some.' What beliefs, habits, or distractions in your life act as opposing forms that prevent you from attaining clear certainty in your faith? Are you willing to actively discard them?
?
Consider a time when you arrived at a conviction about a matter of religion. Looking back, can you identify the two movements—the gathering of evidence and the arranging of that evidence? How does recognizing the structured nature of reasoning affect the confidence you have in your beliefs?
